2:32:1885:0:14:72:531:700:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:How Does Your Kindergarten Classroom Affect Your Earnings? Evidence From Project STAR
2:33:1970:14:14:72:547:686:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Raj Chetty, John N. Friedman, Nathaniel Hilger, Emmanuel Saez, Diane Whitmore Schanzenbach,
2:34:2061:14:14:72:159:672:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:and Danny Yagan
2:35:2076:14:14:72:234:658:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:NBER Working Paper No. 16381
2:36:2104:14:14:72:260:644:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:September 2010, Revised August 2011
2:37:2139:14:28:72:143:630:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:JEL No. H0,J0
2:38:2152:28:28:273:338:602:12:Times:12:Times:ABSTRACT
2:39:2160:28:14:72:540:574:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:In Project STAR, 11,571 students in Tennessee and their teachers were randomly assigned to classrooms
2:40:2261:14:14:72:540:560:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:within their schools from kindergarten to third grade. This paper evaluates the long-term impacts of
2:41:2361:14:14:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:STAR by linking the experimental data to administrative records. We first demonstrate that kindergarten
2:42:2464:14:14:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:test scores are highly correlated with outcomes such as earnings at age 27, college attendance, home
2:43:2564:14:14:72:540:518:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:ownership, and retirement savings. We then document four sets of experimental impacts. First, students
2:44:2666:14:14:72:540:504:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:in small classes are significantly more likely to attend college and exhibit improvements on other outcomes.
2:45:2774:14:14:72:540:490:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Class size does not have a significant effect on earnings at age 27, but this effect is imprecisely estimated.
2:46:2884:14:14:72:540:476:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Second, students who had a more experienced teacher in kindergarten have higher earnings. Third,
2:47:2980:14:14:72:540:462:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:an analysis of variance reveals significant classroom effects on earnings. Students who were randomly
2:48:3081:14:14:72:540:448:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:assigned to higher quality classrooms in grades K-3 – as measured by classmates' end-of-class test
2:49:3179:14:14:72:540:434:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:scores – have higher earnings, college attendance rates, and other outcomes. Finally, the effects of
2:50:3279:14:42:72:526:420:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:class quality fade out on test scores in later grades but gains in non-cognitive measures persist.
2:51:3377:42:14:72:124:378:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Raj Chetty
2:52:3387:14:14:72:198:364:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Department of Economics

3:92:4153:0:32:256:356:709:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:I.  Introduction
3:93:4169:32:20:72:540:677:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:What are the long-term impacts of early childhood education?  Evidence on this important policy
3:94:4264:20:20:72:540:657:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:question remains scarce because of a lack of data linking childhood education and outcomes in
3:95:4357:20:21:72:540:637:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:adulthood.  This paper analyzes the long-term impacts of Project STAR, one of the most widely
3:96:4450:21:20:72:540:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:studied education experiments in the United States.  The Student/Teacher Achievement Ratio
3:97:4540:20:20:72:540:596:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(STAR) experiment randomly assigned one cohort of 11,571 students and their teachers to di§erent
3:98:4636:20:21:72:540:576:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classrooms within their schools in grades K-3.  Some students were assigned to small classes (15
3:99:4732:21:20:72:540:555:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:students on average) in grades K-3, while others were assigned to large classes (22 students on
3:100:4827:20:20:72:540:535:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:average).  The experiment was implemented across 79 schools in Tennessee from 1985 to 1989.
3:101:4918:20:20:72:540:515:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Numerous studies have used the STAR experiment to show that class size, teacher quality, and
3:102:5010:20:21:72:540:495:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:peers have signiÖcant causal impacts on test scores (see Schanzenbach 2006 for a review). Whether
3:103:5107:21:20:72:540:474:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:these gains in achievement on standardized tests translate into improvements in adult outcomes
3:104:5201:20:20:72:278:454:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:such as earnings remains an open question.
3:105:5243:20:21:88:539:434:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We link the original STAR data to administrative data from tax returns, allowing us to follow
3:106:5336:21:20:72:540:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:95% of the STAR participants into adulthood.1We use these data to analyze the impacts of STAR
3:107:5429:20:20:72:540:393:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on outcomes ranging from college attendance and earnings to retirement savings, home ownership,
3:108:5524:20:21:72:540:373:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and marriage.  We begin by documenting the strong correlation between kindergarten test scores
3:109:5618:21:20:72:540:352:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and adult outcomes. A one percentile increase in end-of-kindergarten (KG) test scores is associated
3:110:5717:20:20:72:540:332:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:with a $132 increase in wage earnings at age 27 in the raw data, and a $94 increase after controlling
3:111:5818:20:21:72:540:312:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for parental characteristics.   Several other adult outcomes ñ such as college attendance rates,
3:112:5914:21:20:72:540:291:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:quality of college attended, home ownership, and 401(k) savings ñ are also all highly correlated
3:113:6010:20:20:72:540:271:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:with kindergarten test scores.  These strong correlations motivate the main question of the paper:
3:114:6108:20:21:72:543:251:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:do classroom environments that raise test scores ñ such as smaller classes and better teachers ñ
3:115:6204:21:20:72:312:230:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:cause analogous improvements in adult outcomes?
3:116:6251:20:20:88:539:210:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Our analysis of the experimental impacts combines two empirical strategies. First, we study the
3:117:6346:20:21:72:540:190:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impacts of observable classroom characteristics. We analyze the impacts of class size using the same
3:118:6446:21:20:72:540:169:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:intent-to-treat speciÖcations as Krueger (1999), who showed that students in small classes scored
3:119:6543:20:20:72:540:149:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:higher on standardized tests.  We Önd that students assigned to small classes are 1.8 percentage
3:120:6639:20:16:72:540:129:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:points more likely to be enrolled in college at age 20, a signiÖcant improvement relative to the mean
3:121:6740:16:15:84:539:113:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:1The data for this project were analyzed through a program developed by the Statistics of Income (SOI) Division
3:122:6851:15:11:72:540:98:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:at the U.S. Internal Revenue Service to support research into the e§ects of tax policy on economic and social outcomes
3:123:6969:11:0:72:275:87:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:and improve the administration of the tax system.
4:124:7018:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:college attendance rate of 26.4% at age 20 in the sample.  We do not Önd signiÖcant di§erences in
4:125:7115:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings at age 27 between students who were in small and large classes, although these earnings
4:126:7211:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impacts are imprecisely estimated.  Students in small classes also exhibit statistically signiÖcant
4:127:7310:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:improvements on a summary index of the other outcomes we examine (home ownership, 401(k)
4:128:7398:21:20:72:463:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:savings, mobility rates, percent college graduate in ZIP code, and marital status).
4:129:7481:20:20:88:539:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We study variation across classrooms along other observable dimensions, such as teacher and
4:130:7572:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:peer characteristics, using a similar approach.  Prior studies (e.g. Krueger 1999) have shown that
4:131:7670:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:STAR students with more experienced teachers score higher on tests.  We Önd similar impacts
4:132:7761:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on earnings.  Students randomly assigned to a KG teacher with more than 10 years of experience
4:133:7855:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earn an extra$1;093(6.9% of mean income) on average at age 27 relative to students with less
4:134:7947:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:experienced teachers.2We also test whether observable peer characteristics have long-term impacts
4:135:8044:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by regressing earnings on the fraction of low-income, female, and black peers in KG. These peer
4:136:8139:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impacts are not signiÖcant, but are very imprecisely estimated because of the limited variation in
4:137:8237:21:20:72:253:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:peer characteristics across classrooms.
4:138:8276:20:20:88:539:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Because we have few measures of observable classroom characteristics, we turn to a second
4:139:8365:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:empirical strategy that captures both observed and unobserved aspects of classrooms.  We use an
4:140:8460:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:analysis of variance approach analogous to that in the teacher e§ects literature to test whether
4:141:8556:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings are clustered by kindergarten classroom.  Because we observe each teacher only once
4:142:8648:20:21:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in our data, we can only estimate ìclass e§ectsî ñ the combined e§ect of teachers, peers, and
4:143:8741:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:any class-level shock ñby exploiting random assignment to KG classrooms of both students and
4:144:8833:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:teachers.  Intuitively, we test whether earnings vary across KG classes by more than what would
4:145:8928:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:be predicted by random variation in student abilities.  AnFtest rejects the null hypothesis that
4:146:9024:21:20:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:KG classroom assignment has no e§ect on earnings.  The standard deviation of class e§ects on
4:147:9116:20:20:72:540:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:annual earnings is approximately 10% of mean earnings, highlighting the large stakes at play in
4:148:9211:20:21:72:198:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:early childhood education.
4:149:9237:21:20:88:539:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The analysis of variance shows that kindergarten classroom assignment has signiÖcant impacts
4:150:9329:20:20:72:540:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on earnings, but it does not tell us whether classrooms that improve scores also generate earnings
4:151:9427:20:21:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:gains.  That is, are class e§ects on earnings correlated with class e§ects on scores?  To analyze
4:152:9524:21:20:72:540:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:this question, we proxy for each studentís KG ìclass qualityî by the average test scores of his
4:153:9619:20:16:72:540:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classmates at the end of kindergarten.  We show that end-of-class peer test scores are an omnibus
4:154:9716:16:15:84:539:103:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:2Because teacher experience is correlated with many other unobserved attributes ñ such as attachment to the
4:155:9823:15:11:72:540:88:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:teaching profession ñwe cannot conclude that increasing teacher experience would improve student outcomes.  This
4:156:9935:11:0:72:483:77:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:evidence simply establishes that a studentís KG teacher has e§ects on his or her earnings as an adult.
5:157:10038:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:measure of class quality because they capture peer e§ects, teacher e§ects, and all other classroom
5:158:10136:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:characteristics that a§ect test scores.  Using this measure, we Önd that kindergarten class quality
5:159:10235:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:has signiÖcant impacts on both test scores and earnings. Students randomly assigned to a classroom
5:160:10333:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:that is one standard deviation higher in quality earn 3% more at age 27.  Students assigned to
5:161:10427:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:higher quality classes are also signiÖcantly more likely to attend college, enroll in higher quality
5:162:10527:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:colleges, and exhibit improvements in the summary index of other outcomes.  The class quality
5:163:10620:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impacts are similar for students who entered the experiment in grades 1-3 and were randomized
5:164:10713:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:into classes at that point.  Hence, the Öndings of this paper should be viewed as evidence on the
5:165:10810:20:20:72:486:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:long-term impacts of early childhood education rather than kindergarten in particular.
5:166:10896:20:21:88:539:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Our analysis of ìclass qualityîmust be interpreted very carefully. The purpose of this analysis
5:167:10991:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is to detect clustering in outcomes at the classroom level: are a childís outcomes correlated with
5:168:11089:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:his peersíoutcomes?  Although we test for such clustering by regressing own scores and earnings
5:169:11184:20:21:72:539:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on peer test scores, we emphasize that such regressions arenotintended to detect peer e§ects.
5:170:11277:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Because we use post-intervention peer scores as the regressor, these scores incorporate the impacts
5:171:11376:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of peer quality, teacher quality, and any random class-level shock (such as noise from construction
5:172:11475:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:outside the classroom).  The correlation between own outcomes and peer scores could be due to
5:173:11568:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:any of these factors.  Our analysis shows that the classroom a student was assigned to in early
5:174:11663:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:childhood matters for outcomes 20 years later, but does not shed light on which speciÖc factors
5:175:11758:20:21:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:should be manipulated to improve adult outcomes.  Further research on which factors contribute
5:176:11852:21:20:72:493:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to high ìclass qualityîwould be extremely valuable in light of the results reported here.
5:177:11941:20:20:88:539:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The impacts of early childhood class assignment on adult outcomes may be particularly surpris-
5:178:12035:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ing because the impacts on test scores ìfade outîrapidly.  The impacts of class size on test scores
5:179:12134:21:20:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:become statistically insigniÖcant by grade 8 (Krueger and Whitmore 2001), as do the impacts of
5:180:12228:20:20:72:540:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:class quality on test scores.  Why do the impacts of early childhood education fade out on test
5:181:12323:20:21:72:540:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:scores but re-emerge in adulthood? We Önd some suggestive evidence that part of the explanation
5:182:12418:21:20:72:540:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:may be non-cognitive skills. We Önd that KG class quality has signiÖcant impacts on non-cognitive
5:183:12515:20:20:72:540:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:measures in 4th and 8th grade such as e§ort, initiative, and lack of disruptive behavior.  These
5:184:12611:20:21:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:non-cognitive measures are highly correlated with earnings even conditional on test scores but are
5:185:12709:21:20:72:540:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:not signiÖcant predictors of future standardized test scores. These results suggest that high quality
5:186:12810:20:20:72:540:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:KG classrooms may build non-cognitive skills that have returns in the labor market but do not
5:187:12903:20:20:72:540:99:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:improve performance on standardized tests. While this evidence is far from conclusive, it highlights
5:188:13003:20:0:72:366:79:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the value of further empirical research on non-cognitive skills.
6:189:13068:0:21:88:539:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In addition to the extensive literature on the impacts of STAR on test scores, our study builds
6:190:13163:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on and contributes to a recent literature investigating selected long-term impacts of class size in
6:191:13262:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the STAR experiment.  These studies have shown that students assigned to small classes are more
6:192:13357:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:likely to complete high school (Finn, Gerber, and Boyd-Zaharias 2005) and take the SAT or ACT
6:193:13450:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:college entrance exams (Krueger and Whitmore 2001) and are less likely to be arrested for crime
6:194:13545:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(Krueger and Whitmore 2001). Most recently, Muennig et al. (2010) report that students in small
6:195:13640:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classes have higher mortality rates, a Önding that we do not obtain in our data as we discuss below.
6:196:13740:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We contribute to this literature by providing a uniÖed evaluation of several outcomes, including
6:197:13836:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the Örst analysis of earnings, and by examining the impacts of teachers, peers, and other attributes
6:198:13936:20:21:72:268:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of the classroom in addition to class size.
6:199:13979:21:20:88:539:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Our results also complement the Öndings of studies on the long-term impacts of other early
6:200:14069:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:childhood interventions, such as the Perry and Abecederian preschool demonstrations and the
6:201:14160:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Head Start program, which also Önd lasting impacts on adult outcomes despite fade-out on test
6:202:14253:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:scores (see Almond and Currie 2010 for a review).  We show that a better classroom environment
6:203:14347:20:20:72:526:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:from ages 5-8 can have substantial long-term beneÖts even without intervention at earlier ages.
6:204:14442:20:21:88:539:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The paper is organized as follows.  In Section II, we review the STAR experimental design
6:205:14531:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and address potential threats to the validity of the experiment.  Section III documents the cross-
6:206:14629:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:sectional correlation between test scores and adult outcomes.  Section IV analyzes the impacts of
6:207:14726:20:21:72:543:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:observable characteristics of classrooms ñ size, teacher characteristics, and peer characteristics ñ
6:208:14826:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on adult outcomes.  In Section V, we study class e§ects more broadly, incorporating unobservable
6:209:14922:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:aspects of class quality.   Section VI documents the fade-out and re-emergence e§ects and the
6:210:15015:20:35:72:482:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:potential role of non-cognitive skills in explaining this pattern.  Section VI concludes.
6:211:15104:35:24:196:415:247:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:II.  Experimental Design and Data
6:212:15137:24:28:72:261:223:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:II.A.  Background on Project STAR
6:213:15170:28:20:72:540:195:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Word et al. (1990), Krueger (1999), and Finn et al. (2007) provide a comprehensive summary of
6:214:15263:20:20:72:540:175:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Project STAR; here, we brieáy review the features of the STAR experiment most relevant for our
6:215:15357:20:21:72:540:155:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:analysis.  The STAR experiment was conducted at 79 schools across the state of Tennessee over
6:216:15450:21:20:72:540:134:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:four years.  The program oversampled lower-income schools, and thus the STAR sample exhibits
6:217:15542:20:20:72:540:114:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:lower socioeconomic characteristics than the state of Tennessee and the U.S. population as a whole.
6:218:15641:20:21:88:539:94:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In the 1985-86 school year, 6,323 kindergarten students in participating schools were randomly
6:219:15735:21:0:72:540:73:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:assigned to a small (target size 13-17 students) or regular-sized (20-25 students) class within their
7:220:15837:0:21:72:539:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:schools.3Students were intended to remain in the same class type (small vs. large) through 3rd
7:221:15931:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:grade, at which point all students would return to regular classes for 4th grade and subsequent years.
7:222:16033:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:As the initial cohort of kindergarten students advanced across grade levels, there was substantial
7:223:16131:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:attrition because students who moved away from a participating school or were retained in grade
7:224:16226:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:no longer received treatment.  In addition, because kindergarten was not mandatory and due to
7:225:16319:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:normal residential mobility, many children joined the initial cohort at the participating schools after
7:226:16422:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:kindergarten. A total of 5,248 students entered the participating schools in grades 1-3. These new
7:227:16520:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:entrants were randomly assigned to classrooms within school upon entry.  Thus all students were
7:228:16615:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:randomized to classrooms within school upon entry, regardless of the entry grade. As a result, the
7:229:16713:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:randomization pool is school-by-entry-grade, and we include school-by-entry-grade Öxed e§ects in
7:230:16809:21:20:72:226:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:all experimental analyzes below.
7:231:16841:20:20:88:539:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Upon entry into one of the 79 schools, the study design randomly assigned students not only
7:232:16932:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to class type (small vs. large) but also to a classroom within each type (if there were multiple
7:233:17028:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classrooms per type, as was the case in 50 of the 79 schools). Teachers were also randomly assigned
7:234:17127:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to classrooms.  Unfortunately, the exact protocol of randomization into speciÖc classrooms was
7:235:17221:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:not clearly documented in any of the o¢ cial STAR reports, where the emphasis was instead the
7:236:17314:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:random assignment into class type rather than classroom (Word et al. 1990). We present statistical
7:237:17412:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:evidence conÖrming that both students and teachers indeed appear to be randomly assigned directly
7:238:17509:20:21:72:459:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to classrooms upon entry into the STAR project, as the original designers attest.
7:239:17590:21:20:88:539:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:As in any Öeld experiment, there were some deviations from the experimental protocol.  In
7:240:17679:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:particular, some students moved from large to small classes and vice versa.  To account for such
7:241:17775:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:potentially non-random sorting, we adopt the standard approach taken in the literature and assign
7:242:17872:21:20:72:407:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:treatment status based on initial random assignment (intent-to-treat).
7:243:17942:20:20:88:539:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In each year, students were administered the grade-appropriate Stanford Achievement Test, a
7:244:18033:20:21:72:540:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:multiple choice test that measures performance in math and reading.  These tests were given only
7:245:18129:21:20:72:540:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to students participating in STAR, as the regular statewide testing program did not extend to the
7:246:18226:20:20:72:539:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:early grades.4Following Krueger (1999), we standardize the math and reading scale scores in each
7:247:18322:20:16:71:539:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:grade by computing the scale scoreís corresponding percentile rank in the distribution for students
7:248:18421:16:15:84:539:144:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:3There was also a third treatment group: regular sized class with a full-time teacherís aide. This was a relatively
7:249:18536:15:11:71:539:129:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:minor intervention, since all regular classes were already assigned a 1/3 time teacherís aide. Prior studies of STAR
7:250:18652:11:11:71:539:118:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:Önd no impact of a full-time teacherís aide on test scores.  We follow the convention in the literature and group the
7:251:18769:11:7:71:294:107:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:regular and regular plus aide class treatments together.
7:252:18825:7:15:84:539:100:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:4These K-3 test scores contain considerable predictive content.  As reported in Krueger Whitmore (2001), the
7:253:18933:15:11:71:539:85:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:correlation between test scores in grades g and g+1 is 0.65 for KG and 0.80 for each grade 1-3.  The values for grades
7:254:19051:11:0:71:474:74:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:4-7 lie between 0.83 and 0.88, suggesting that the K-3 test scores contain similar predictive content.
8:255:19154:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in large classes.  We then assign the appropriate percentile rank to students in small classes and
8:256:19252:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:take the average across math and reading percentile ranks. Note that this percentile measure is a
8:257:19349:20:29:72:288:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ranking of studentswithinthe STAR sample.
8:258:19390:29:28:72:336:639:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:II.B.  Variable DeÖnitions and Summary Statistics
8:259:19439:28:20:72:540:611:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We measure adult outcomes of Project STAR participants using administrative data from United
8:260:19531:20:20:72:540:591:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:States tax records. 95.0% of STAR records were linked to the tax data using an algorithm based on
8:261:19628:20:21:72:540:571:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:standard identiÖers (SSN, date of birth, gender, and names) that is described in Online Appendix
8:262:19724:21:20:72:87:550:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:A.5
8:263:19727:20:20:88:539:530:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We obtain data on students and their parents from federal tax forms such as 1040 individual
8:264:19818:20:21:72:540:510:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:income tax returns.  Information from 1040ís is available from 1996-2008.  Approximately 10%
8:265:19910:21:20:72:540:489:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of adults do not Öle individual income tax returns in a given year.  We use third-party reports
8:266:20005:20:20:72:540:469:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to obtain information such as wage earnings (form W-2) and college attendance (form 1098-T) for
8:267:20100:20:21:72:540:449:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:all individuals, including those who do not Öle 1040s.  Data from these third-party reports are
8:268:20195:21:20:72:540:428:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:available since 1999.  The year always refers to the tax year (i.e., the calendar year in which the
8:269:20294:20:20:72:540:408:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:income is earned or the college expense incurred). In most cases, tax returns for tax yeartare Öled
8:270:20393:20:21:72:539:388:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:during the calendar yeart+1. The analysis dataset combines selected variables from individual tax
8:271:20490:21:20:72:540:367:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:returns, third party reports, and information from the STAR database, with individual identiÖers
8:272:20586:20:20:72:236:347:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:removed to protect conÖdentiality.
8:273:20620:20:21:88:539:327:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We now describe how each of the adult outcome measures and control variables used in the
8:274:20708:21:20:72:540:306:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:empirical analysis is constructed.  Table I reports summary statistics for these variables for the
8:275:20806:20:20:72:540:286:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:STAR sample as well as a random 0.25% sample of the US population born in the same years
8:276:20894:20:21:72:131:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(1979-1980).
8:277:20906:21:20:88:539:245:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:Earnings.The individual earnings data come from W-2 forms, yielding information on earnings
8:278:20997:20:20:72:539:225:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for both Ölers and non-Ölers.6We deÖne earnings in each year as the sum of earnings on all W-2
8:279:21091:20:20:72:540:205:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:forms Öled on an individualís behalf.  We express all monetary variables in 2009 dollars, adjusting
8:280:21190:20:21:72:540:185:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for ináation using the Consumer Price Index.  We cap earnings in each year at $100,000 to reduce
8:281:21286:21:16:72:540:164:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the ináuence of outliers; fewer than 1% of individuals in the STAR sample report earnings above
8:282:21381:16:15:84:539:148:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:5All appendix material is available as an on-line appendix posted as supplementary material to the article. Note
8:283:21493:15:10:72:540:133:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:that the matching algorithm was su¢ ciently precise that it uncovered 28 cases in the original STAR dataset that were
8:284:21610:10:8:72:530:123:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:a single split observation or duplicate records. After consolidating these records, we are left with 11,571 students.
8:285:21727:8:14:84:539:115:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:6We obtain similar results using household adjusted gross income reported on individual tax returns. We focus on
8:286:21839:14:11:72:540:101:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:the W-2 measure because it provides a consistent deÖnition of individual wage earnings for both Ölers and non-Ölers.
8:287:21955:11:0:72:415:90:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:One limitation of the W-2 easure is that it does not include self-employment income.
9:288:22040:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:$100,000 in a given year. To increase precision, we typically use average (ináation indexed) earnings
9:289:22141:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:from year 2005 to 2007 as an outcome measure. The mean individual earnings for the STAR sample
9:290:22235:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in 2005-2007 (when the STAR students are 25-27 years old) is $15,912.  This earnings measure
9:291:22327:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:includes zeros for the 13.9% of STAR students who report no income 2005-2007. The mean level of
9:292:22422:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings in the STAR sample is lower than in the same cohort in the U.S. population, as expected
9:293:22518:20:20:72:373:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:given that Project STAR targeted more disadvantaged schools.
9:294:22578:20:21:88:539:587:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:College Attendance.Higher education institutions eligible for federal Önancial aid ñTitle IV
9:295:22670:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:institutions ñare required to Öle 1098-T forms that report tuition payments or scholarships received
9:296:22770:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for every student.7Title IV institutions include all colleges and universities as well as vocational
9:297:22870:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:schools and other postsecondary institutions.  Comparisons to other data sources indicate that
9:298:22964:21:20:72:539:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1098-T forms accurately capture US college enrollment.8We have data on college attendance from
9:299:23058:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1098-T forms for all students in our sample since 1999, when the STAR students were 19 years
9:300:23150:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:old.  We deÖne college attendance as an indicator for having one or more 1098-T forms Öled on
9:301:23243:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:oneís behalf in a given year.  In the STAR sample, 26.4% of students are enrolled in college at age
9:302:23342:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:20 (year 2000). 45.5% of students are enrolled in college at some point between 1999 and 2007,
9:303:23436:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:compared with 57.1% in the same cohort of the U.S. population. Because the data are based purely
9:304:23532:21:20:72:515:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on tuition payments, we have no information about college completion or degree attainment.
9:305:23622:20:20:88:539:363:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:College Quality.Using the institutional identiÖers on the 1098-T forms, we construct an
9:306:23709:20:21:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings-based index of college quality as follows. First, using the full population of all individuals
9:307:23812:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in the United States aged 20 on 12/31/1999 and all 1098-T forms for year 1999, we group individuals
9:308:23911:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by the higher education institution they attended in 1999.  This sample contains over 1.4 million
9:309:24008:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:individuals.9We take a 1% sample of those not attending a higher education institution in 1999,
9:310:24103:21:20:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:comprising another 27,733 individuals, and pool them together in a separate ìno collegeîcategory.
9:311:24200:20:20:72:540:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Next, we compute average earnings of the students in 2007 when they are aged 28 by grouping
9:312:24291:20:21:72:540:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:students according to the educational institution they attended in 1999.   This earnings-based
9:313:24385:21:15:72:540:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:index of college quality is highly correlated with the US News ranking of the best 125 colleges and
9:314:24484:15:15:84:539:185:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:7These forms are used to administer the Hope and Lifetime Learning education tax credits created by the Taxpayer
9:315:24596:15:11:72:540:170:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:Relief Act of 1997.  Colleges are not required to Öle 1098-T forms for students whose qualiÖed tuition and related
9:316:24710:11:11:72:540:159:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:expenses are waived or paid entirely with scholarships or grants; however, in many instances the forms are available
9:317:24826:11:7:72:372:148:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:even for such cases, perhaps because of automation at the university level.
9:318:24901:7:15:84:539:141:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:8In 2009, 27.4 million 1098-T forms were issued (Internal Revenue Service, 2010).  According to the Current
9:319:25008:15:11:72:540:126:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:Population Survey (US Census Bureau, 2010, Tables V and VI), in October 2008, there were 22.6 million students in
9:320:25121:11:11:72:540:115:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:the U.S. (13.2 million full time, 5.4 million part-time, and 4 million vocational).  As an individual can be a student
9:321:25239:11:11:72:540:104:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:at some point during the year but not in October and can receive a 1098-T form from more than one institution, the
9:322:25353:11:7:72:534:93:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:number of 1098-T forms for the calendar year should indeed be higher than the number of students as of October.
9:323:25464:7:14:84:539:86:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:9Individuals who attended more than one institution in 1999 are counted as students at all institutions they
9:324:25572:14:0:72:110:72:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:attended.
10:325:25582:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:universities: the correlation coe¢ cient of our measure and the log US news rank is 0.75.  The
10:326:25676:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:advantages of our index are that while the US News ranking only covers the top 125 institutions,
10:327:25772:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ours covers all higher education institutions in the U.S. and provides a simple cardinal metric for
10:328:25871:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:college quality.  Among colleges attended by STAR students, the average value of our earnings
10:329:25964:21:20:72:539:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:index is $35,080 for four-year colleges and $26,920 for two-year colleges.10For students who did
10:330:26060:20:20:72:332:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:not attend college, the imputed mean wage is $16,475.
10:331:26113:20:21:88:539:587:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:Other Outcomes.We identify spouses using information from 1040 forms.  For individuals
10:332:26199:21:20:71:539:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:who Öle tax returns, we deÖne an indicator for marriage based on whether the tax return is Öled
10:333:26294:20:20:71:539:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:jointly.  We code non-Ölers as single because most non-Ölers in the U.S. who are not receiving
10:334:26388:20:21:71:539:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Social Security beneÖts are single (Cilke 1998, Table I). We deÖne a measure of ever being married
10:335:26486:21:20:71:539:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by age 27 as an indicator for ever Öling a joint tax return in any year between 1999 and 2007.  By

10:337:26659:20:21:88:539:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We measure retirement savings using contributions to 401(k) accounts reported on W-2 forms
10:338:26749:21:20:71:539:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:from 1999-2007.  28.2% of individuals in the sample make a 401(k) contribution at some point
10:339:26841:20:20:71:539:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:during this period.  We measure home ownership using data from the 1098 form, a third party
10:340:26932:20:21:71:539:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:report Öled by lenders to report mortgage interest payments. We include the few individuals who
10:341:27027:21:20:71:539:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:report a mortgage deduction on their 1040 forms but do not have 1098ís as homeowners. We deÖne
10:342:27121:20:20:71:539:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:any individual who has a mortgage interest deduction at any point between 1999 and 2007 as a
10:343:27213:20:21:71:539:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:homeowner. Note that this measure of home ownership does not cover individuals who own homes
10:344:27305:21:20:71:539:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:without a mortgage, which is rare among individuals younger than 27.  By our measure, 30.8% of
10:345:27399:20:20:71:539:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:individuals own a home by age 27.  We use data from 1040 forms to identify each householdís ZIP
10:346:27494:20:21:71:539:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:code of residence in each year. For non-Ölers, we use the ZIP code of the address to which the W-2
10:347:27592:21:20:71:539:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:form was mailed.  If an individual did not Öle and has no W-2 in a given year, we impute current
10:348:27688:20:20:71:539:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ZIP code as the last observed ZIP code. We deÖne a measure of cross-state mobility by an indicator
10:349:27786:20:21:71:539:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for whether the individual ever lived outside Tennessee between 1999 and 2007.  27.5% of STAR
10:350:27879:21:20:71:539:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:students lived outside Tennessee at some point between age 19 and 27.  We construct a measure
10:351:27972:20:20:71:539:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of neighborhood quality using data on the percentage of college graduates in the individualís 2007
10:352:28070:20:21:71:539:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ZIP code from the 2000 Census.  On average, STAR students lived in 2007 in neighborhoods with
10:353:28163:21:20:71:187:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:17.6% college graduates.
10:354:28187:20:16:88:539:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We observe dates of birth and death until the end of 2009 as recorded by the Social Security
10:355:28279:16:15:80:539:103:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:10For the small fraction of STAR students who attend more than one college in a single year, we deÖne college
10:356:28388:15:0:71:458:88:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:quality based on the college that received the largest tuition payments on behalf of the student.
11:357:28486:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Administration. We deÖne each STAR participantís age at kindergarten entry as the studentís age
11:358:28581:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(in days divided by 365.25) as of September 1, 1985.  Virtually all students in STAR were born in

11:361:28865:21:20:72:168:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is deceased by 2009.
11:362:28885:20:20:88:539:607:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:Parent Characteristics.We link STAR children to their parents by Önding the earliest 1040
11:363:28974:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:form from 1996-2008 on which the STAR student was claimed as dependents.  Most matches were
11:364:29065:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:found on 1040 forms for the tax year 1996, when the STAR children were 16.  We identify parents
11:365:29160:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for 86% of the STAR students in our linked dataset.  The remaining students are likely to have
11:366:29254:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:parents who did not Öle tax returns in the early years of the sample when they could have claimed
11:367:29351:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:their child as a dependent, making it impossible to link the children to their parents.  Note that
11:368:29449:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:this deÖnition of parents is based on who claims the child as a dependent, and thus may not reáect
11:369:29547:20:21:72:230:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the biological parent of the child.
11:370:29582:21:20:88:539:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We deÖne parental household income as average Adjusted Gross Income (capped at $252,000,
11:371:29670:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the 99th percentile in our sample) from 1996-1998, when the children were 16-18 years old.  For
11:372:29765:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:years in which parents did not Öle, we deÖne parental household income as zero.  For divorced
11:373:29858:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:parents, this income measure captures the total resources available to the household claiming the
11:374:29955:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:child as a dependent (including any alimony payments), rather than the sum of the individual
11:375:30047:20:21:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:incomes of the two parents.  By this measure, mean parent income is $48,010 (in 2009 dollars) for
11:376:30144:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:STAR students whom we are able to link to parents.  We deÖne marital status, home ownership,
11:377:30236:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and 401(k) saving as indicators for whether the parent who claims the STAR child ever Öles a joint
11:378:30334:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tax return, has a mortgage interest payment, or makes a 401(k) contribution over the period for
11:379:30429:21:20:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:which relevant data are available.  We deÖne motherís age at childís birth using data from Social
11:380:30526:20:20:72:540:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Security Administration records on birth dates for parents and children.  For single parents, we
11:381:30622:20:21:72:540:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:deÖne the motherís age at childís birth using the age of the Öler who claimed the child, who is
11:382:30717:21:20:72:539:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:typically the mother but is sometimes the father or another relative.11By this measure, mothers
11:383:30812:20:20:72:540:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:are on average 25.0 years old when they give birth to a child in the STAR sample.  When a child
11:384:30907:20:16:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:cannot be matched to a parent, we deÖne all parental characteristics as zero, and we always include
11:385:31006:16:27:80:539:144:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:10Alternative deÖnitions of income for non-Ölers ñsuch as income reported on W-2ís starting in 1999 ñyield very
11:386:31117:27:7:72:230:117:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:similar results to those reported below.
11:387:31157:7:15:80:539:110:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:11We deÖne the motherís age at childís birth as missing for 471 observations in which the implied motherís age
11:388:31267:15:11:72:540:95:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:at birth based on the claiming parentís date of birth is below 13 or above 65.  These are typically cases where the
11:389:31382:11:0:72:351:84:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:parent does not have an accurate birth date recorded in the SSA Öle.
12:390:31451:0:21:72:448:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:a dummy for missing parents in regressions that include parent characteristics.
12:391:31530:21:20:88:540:688:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:Background Variables from STAR.In addition to classroom assignment and test score variables,
12:392:31622:20:20:71:539:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:we use some demographic information from the STAR database in our analysis.  This includes
12:393:31712:20:21:71:539:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:gender, race (an indicator for being black), and whether the student ever received free or reduced
12:394:31810:21:20:71:539:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:price lunch during the experiment.  36% of the STAR sample are black and 60% are eligible for
12:395:31903:20:20:71:539:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:free or reduced-price lunches.  Finally, we use data on teacher characteristics ñexperience, race,
12:396:32001:20:21:71:539:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and highest degree ñfrom the STAR database. The average student has a teacher with 10.8 years
12:397:32094:21:20:71:539:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of experience. 19.5% of kindergarten students have a black teacher, and 35.9% have a teacher with
12:398:32191:20:20:71:203:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:a masterís degree or higher.
12:399:32219:20:21:88:539:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Our analysis dataset contains one observation for each of the 10,992 STAR students we link
12:400:32309:21:20:71:539:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to the tax data.  Each observation contains information on the studentís adult outcomes, parent
12:401:32404:20:20:71:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:characteristics, and classroom characteristics in the grade the studententeredthe STAR project
12:402:32498:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and was randomly assigned to a classroom. Hence, when we pool students across grades, we include
12:403:32594:21:29:72:343:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:test score and classroom data only from the entry grade.
12:404:32650:29:27:72:291:415:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:II.C.  Validity of the Experimental Design
12:405:32692:27:21:72:540:388:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The validity of the causal inferences that follow rests on two assumptions: successful randomization
12:406:32792:21:20:72:540:367:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of students into classrooms and no di§erences in attrition (match rates) across classrooms.  We
12:407:32887:20:20:72:232:347:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:now evaluate each of these issues.
12:408:32921:20:21:88:539:327:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:Randomization into Classrooms.To evaluate whether the randomization protocol was imple-
12:409:33008:21:20:72:540:306:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:mented as designed, we test for balance in pre-determined variables across classrooms. The original
12:410:33107:20:20:72:540:286:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:STAR dataset contains only a few pre-determined variables: age, gender, race, and free-lunch status.
12:411:33207:20:21:72:540:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Although the data are balanced on these characteristics, some skepticism naturally has remained
12:412:33302:21:20:72:356:245:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:because of the coarseness of the variables (Hanushek 2003).
12:413:33361:20:20:88:539:225:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The tax data allow us to improve upon the prior evidence on the validity of randomization
12:414:33450:20:20:72:540:205:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by investigating a wider variety of family background characteristics.   In particular, we check
12:415:33546:20:21:72:540:185:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for balance in the following Öve parental characteristics: household income, 401(k) savings, home
12:416:33643:21:20:72:540:164:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ownership, marital status, and motherís age at childís birth. Although most of these characteristics
12:417:33743:20:20:72:540:144:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:are not measured prior to random assignment in 1985, they are measured prior to the STAR cohortís
12:418:33840:20:21:72:540:124:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:expected graduation from high school and are unlikely to be impacted by the childís classroom
12:419:33933:21:20:72:540:103:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:assignment in grades K-3.  We Örst establish that these parental characteristics are in fact strong
12:420:34032:20:0:72:540:83:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:predictors of student outcomes.  In column 1 of Table II, we regress the childís earnings on the
13:421:34130:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Öve parent characteristics, the studentís age, gender, race, and free-lunch status, and school-by-
13:422:34228:21:20:72:543:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:entry-grade Öxed e§ects. We also include indicators for missing data on certain variables (parentsí
13:423:34327:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:characteristics, motherís age, studentís free lunch status, and studentís race).  The student and
13:424:34424:20:21:72:451:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:parent demographic characteristics are highly signiÖcant predictors of earnings.
13:425:34504:21:20:88:539:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Having identiÖed a set of pre-determined characteristics that predict childrenís future earnings,
13:426:34601:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:we test for balance in these covariates across classrooms. We Örst evaluate randomization into the
13:427:34699:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:small class treatment by regressing an indicator for being assigned to a small class upon entry on
13:428:34797:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the same variables as in column 1.  As shown in column 2 of Table II, none of the demographic
13:429:34890:20:20:72:539:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:characteristics predict the likelihood that a child is assigned to a small class. AnFtest for the joint
13:430:34993:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:signiÖcance of all the pre-determined demographic variables is insigniÖcant (p= 0:26), showing that
13:431:35092:21:20:72:435:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:students in small and large classes have similar demographic characteristics.
13:432:35169:20:20:88:539:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Columns 3-5 of Table II evaluate the random assignment of teachers to classes by regressing
13:433:35260:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:teacher characteristics ñexperience, bachelorís degree, and race ñon the same student and parent
13:434:35356:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:characteristics.  Again, none of the pre-determined variables predict the type of teacher a student

13:452:37005:20:0:88:539:79:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:Selective Attrition.Another threat to the experimental design is di§erential attrition across

14:470:38701:20:20:72:539:363:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f2:0:01percentage points.  This Önding is inconsistent with recent results reported by Muennig et

14:473:38986:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:154 STAR students have died by 2007 while Muennig et al. (2010) Önd 141 deaths in their data.
14:474:39079:20:35:72:477:282:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:The discrepancy between the Öndings might be due to di§erences in match quality.12
14:475:39161:35:31:123:488:247:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:III.  Test Scores and Adult Outcomes in the Cross-Section
14:476:39218:31:20:71:539:216:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We begin by documenting the correlations between test scores and adult outcomes in the cross-
14:477:39311:20:20:71:539:196:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:section to provide a benchmark for assessing the impacts of the randomized interventions.  Figure
14:478:39408:20:21:71:539:176:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Ia documents the association between end-of-kindergarten test scores and mean earnings from age
14:479:39503:21:16:71:539:155:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:25-27.13To construct this Ögure, we bin individuals into twenty equal-width bins (vingtiles) and
14:480:39599:16:15:80:539:139:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:12As 95% of STAR students are matched to the our data and have a valid Social Security Number, we believe
14:481:39704:15:11:71:539:124:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:that deaths are recorded accurately in our sample.  It is unclear why a lower match rate would lead to a systematic
14:482:39819:11:11:71:539:113:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:di§erence in death rates by class size.  However, given the small number of deaths, slight imbalances might generate
14:483:39935:11:7:71:337:102:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:marginally signiÖcant di§erences in death rates across class types.
14:484:40002:7:15:80:539:95:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:13Although individualsíearnings trajectories remain quite steep at age 27, earnings levels from ages 25-27 are highly
14:485:40119:15:0:71:539:80:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:correlated with earnings at later ages (Haider and Solon 2006), a Önding we have conÖrmed with our population wide
15:486:40235:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:plot mean earnings in each bin.  A one percentile point increase in KG test score is associated
15:487:40330:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:with a $132 (0.83%) increase in earnings twenty years later. If one codes the x-axis using national
15:488:40429:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:percentiles on the standardized KG tests instead of within-sample percentiles, the earnings increase
15:489:40529:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is $154 per percentile.  The correlation between KG test score percentiles and earnings is linear
15:490:40626:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and remains signiÖcant even in the tails of the distribution of test scores. However, KG test scores
15:491:40726:20:20:72:539:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:explain only a small share of the variation in adult earnings: the adjustedR2of the regression of
15:492:40823:20:21:72:198:587:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:earnings on scores is 5%.14
15:493:40850:21:20:88:539:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Figures Ib and Ic show that KG test scores are highly predictive of college attendance rates and
15:494:40946:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the quality of the college the student attends, as measured by our earnings-based index of college
15:495:41044:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:quality. To analyze the other adult outcomes in a compact manner, we construct a summary index
15:496:41138:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of Öve outcomes: ever owning a home by 2007, 401(k) savings by 2007, ever married by 2007, ever
15:497:41233:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:living outside Tennessee by 2007, and living in a higher SES neighborhood in 2007 as measured
15:498:41326:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by the percent of college graduates living in the ZIP code.  Following Kling, Liebman, and Katz
15:499:41421:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(2007), we Örst standardize each outcome by subtracting its mean and dividing it by its standard
15:500:41517:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:deviation.  We then sum the Öve standardized outcomes and divide by the standard deviation
15:501:41607:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of the sum to obtain an index that has a standard deviation of 1.  A higher value of the index
15:502:41701:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:represents more desirable outcomes.  Students with higher entry-year test scores have stronger
15:503:41795:20:20:72:431:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:adult outcomes as measured by the summary index, as shown in Figure Id.
15:504:41866:20:21:88:539:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The summary index should be interpreted as a broader measure of success in young adulthood.
15:505:41957:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Some of its elements proxy for future earnings conditional on current income. For example, having
15:506:42054:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:401(k) savings reáects holding a good job that o§ers such beneÖts.  Living outside Tennessee is
15:507:42149:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:a proxy for cross-state mobility, which is typically associated with higher socio-economic status.
15:508:42247:21:20:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:While none of these outcomes are unambiguously positive ñfor instance, marriage or homeownership
15:509:42343:20:20:72:540:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by age 27 could in principle reáect imprudence ñ existing evidence suggests that, on net, these
15:510:42438:20:21:72:540:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:measures are associated with better outcomes.  In our sample, each of the Öve outcomes is highly
15:511:42534:21:20:72:496:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:positively correlated with test scores on its own, as shown in Online Appendix Table III.

22:731:62266:20:20:88:539:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The impact of kindergarten teacher experience on earnings must be interpreted very carefully.
22:732:62359:20:21:71:539:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Our results show that placing a child in a kindergarten class taught by a more experienced teacher
22:733:62457:21:20:71:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:yields improved outcomes.  This Önding doesnotimply that increasing a given teacherís experi-
22:734:62550:20:20:71:539:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ence will improve student outcomes.  The reason is that while teachers were randomly assigned to
22:735:62646:20:21:71:539:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classrooms, experience was not randomly assigned to teachers.  The di§erence in earnings of stu-
22:736:62742:21:20:71:539:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:dents with experienced teachers could be due to the intrinsic characteristics of experienced teachers
22:737:62843:20:20:71:539:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:rather than experience of teachers per se. For instance, teachers with more experience have selected
22:738:62943:20:21:71:539:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to stay in the profession and may be more passionate or more skilled at teaching.  Alternatively,
22:739:63040:21:16:71:539:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:teachers from older cohorts may have been more skilled (Corcoran, Evans, and Schwab 2004, Hoxby
22:740:63135:16:15:80:539:123:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:21In Online Appendix Table XII, we replicate columns 1 and 2 for small and large classes separately to evaluate
22:741:63246:15:11:71:539:108:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:whether teacher experience is more important in managing classrooms with many students.  We Önd some evidence

23:747:63752:20:21:88:539:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The linear relationship between kindergarten teacher experience and scores in the STAR data
23:748:63843:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:stands in contrast to earlier studies that track teachers over time in a panel and Önd that teacher

23:751:64129:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and earnings.  We therefore conclude that early childhood teaching has a causal impact on long

23:753:64318:20:21:88:539:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The few other observable teacher characteristics in the STAR data (degrees, race, and progress

23:758:64784:20:20:88:539:424:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:Peers.Better classmates could create an environment more conducive to learning, leading to
23:759:64874:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:improvements in adult outcomes. To test for such peer e§ects, we follow the standard approach in
23:760:64970:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the recent literature by using linear-in-means regressions speciÖcations.  We include students who
23:761:65068:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:enter in all grades and measure peer characteristics in their Örst, randomly assigned classroom, and
23:762:65168:20:21:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:condition on school-by-entry-grade e§ects. We proxy for peer abilities (z) in equation (2) with the
23:763:65267:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:following exogenous peer characteristics: fraction black, fraction female, fraction eligible for free or
23:764:65371:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reduced-price lunch (a proxy for low income), and mean age. Replicating previous studies, we show
23:765:65468:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in column 5 of Table VI that the fraction of female and low-income peers signiÖcantly predict test
23:766:65566:21:20:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:scores.  Column 6 replicates column 5 with earnings as the dependent variable.  The estimates on
23:767:65662:20:20:72:540:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:all four peer characteristics are very imprecise. For instance, the estimated e§ect of increasing the
23:768:65763:20:21:72:540:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:fraction of low-income peers by 10 percentage points is an earnings loss of $28, but with a standard
23:769:65863:21:20:72:540:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:error of $173. In an attempt to obtain more power, we construct a single index of peer abilities by
23:770:65962:20:20:72:540:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Örst regressing scores on the full set of parent and student demographic characteristics described
23:771:66060:20:21:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:above and then predicting peersíscores using this regression.  However, as column 7 shows, even
23:772:66155:21:20:72:540:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the predicted peer score measure does not yield a precise estimate of peer e§ects on earnings; the
23:773:66253:20:16:72:540:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:95% conÖdence interval for a 1 percentile point improvement in peersípredicted test scores ranges
23:774:66350:16:15:80:539:103:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:22Dee (2004) shows that being assigned to a teacher of the same race raises test scores.  We Önd a positive but
23:775:66461:15:0:72:401:88:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:statistically insigniÖcant impact of having a teacher of the same race on earnings.
24:776:66546:0:21:72:173:709:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:from -$207 to $160.23
24:777:66567:21:20:88:539:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The STAR experiment lacks the power to measure the e§ects of observable peer characteristics
24:778:66659:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on earnings precisely because the experimental design randomized students across classrooms. As a
24:779:66756:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:result, it does not generate signiÖcant variation in mean peer abilities across classes. The standard
24:780:66857:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:deviation of mean predicted peer test scores (removing variation across schools and waves) is less

24:783:67149:21:34:72:507:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:outcomes twenty years later, which are subject to much higher levels of idiosyncratic noise.
24:784:67241:34:31:133:478:532:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:V.  Impacts of Unobservable Classroom Characteristics
24:785:67294:31:21:72:540:501:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Many unobserved aspects of teachers and peers could impact student achievement and adult out-
24:786:67387:21:20:72:540:480:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:comes.  For instance, some teachers may generate greater enthusiasm among students or some
24:787:67477:20:20:72:540:460:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:peers might be particularly disruptive.  To test whether such unobservable aspects of class quality
24:788:67576:20:21:72:540:440:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:have long-term impacts, we estimate the parameters of a correlated random e§ects model.  In
24:789:67667:21:20:72:540:419:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:particular, we test for ìclass e§ectsî on scores and earnings by exploiting random assignment to
24:790:67763:20:20:72:540:399:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classrooms.  These class e§ects include the e§ects of teachers, peers, and any class-level shocks.
24:791:67861:20:29:72:403:379:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We formalize our estimation strategy using a simple empirical model.

26:854:71691:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:test for class e§ects on earnings (\fvar(zcn) +var(zYcn)>0) and a regression-based method to test

26:866:72721:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:environments that raise test scores also improve adult outcomes?  This is an important question
26:867:72816:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:because the impacts of most educational policies can be measured only by test scores in the short
26:868:72913:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:run.  Second, in the STAR data, roughly half the students enter in grades 1-3 and are randomly
26:869:73007:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:assigned to classrooms at that point.  Because only a small number of students enter each school
26:870:73103:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in each of these later grades, we do not have the power to detect class e§ects in later grades and
26:871:73201:20:20:72:336:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:therefore do not include these students in the ANOVA.

28:967:77617:21:20:88:539:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We include students who enter STAR in later grades when estimating equation (10) by deÖning

28:969:77805:20:21:72:540:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:studentís grade in the year she entered a STAR school.  To maximize precision, we include all
28:970:77898:21:20:72:539:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:peers (including those who had entered in earlier grades) when deÖning-s-icnfor new entrants.

28:972:78083:20:21:72:538:160:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f8:to their Örst classroom ñas it does for kindergarten entrants.28With this deÖnition of-s-icn,bLM
28:973:78179:21:16:72:540:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:measures the extent to which class quality in the initial class of entry (weighted by the entry rates
28:974:78280:16:15:80:539:123:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:27We use the di§erence between peer scores in the class and the school (rather than simply using classmatesíscores)
28:975:78395:15:7:72:506:108:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:to address the Önite-sample bias in small peer groups identiÖed by Guryan, Kroft, and Notowidigdo (2009).
28:976:78500:7:15:80:539:101:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:28For entrants in grades 1-3, there can be additional noise in the class quality measure because students who had

28:978:78728:11:0:72:352:75:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:entering student ability, it generates only additional attenuation bias.
29:979:78802:0:21:72:265:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:across the four grades) a§ects outcomes.
29:980:78842:21:20:88:539:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:An alternative approach to measuring the covariance between class e§ects on scores and earnings
29:981:78937:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is to use an instrumental variables strategy, regressing earnings on test scores and instrumenting
29:982:79035:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for scores with classroom Öxed e§ects. Because the Ötted values from the Örst stage regression are
29:983:79133:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:just mean test scores by classroom, the coe¢ cient obtained from this TSLS regression coincides
29:984:79228:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:withbMwhen we run equation (8).  The TSLS estimate of\fis upward biased because the own
29:985:79315:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:observation is included in both mean scores and mean earnings, which is the well known weak
29:986:79406:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:instruments problem.  The weak instruments literature has developed various techniques to deal
29:987:79500:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:with this bias, including (a) jackknife IV (Angrist, Imbens, and Krueger 1999), which solves the
29:988:79596:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:problem by omitting the own observation when forming the instrument; (b) split-sample IV (Angrist
29:989:79693:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and Krueger 1995), which randomly splits classes into two and only uses mean scores in the other
29:990:79789:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:half of the class as an instrument; and (c) limited information maximum likelihood (LIML), which
29:991:79885:20:21:72:539:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:collapses the parameter space and uses maximum likelihood to obtain a consistent estimate of\f.
29:992:79980:21:20:71:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The estimator forbLMin equation (10) is essentially the reduced-form of the Örst technique, the
29:993:80075:20:20:71:539:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:jackknife IV regression.  We present estimates using the instrumental variable strategies in Online
29:994:80174:20:30:71:369:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Appendix Table XIII to evaluate the robustness of our results.

30:1037:83751:17:15:80:539:143:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:30The clustering of earnings detected by the ANOVA may appear to contradict that fact that clustering standard
30:1038:83861:15:10:71:539:128:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:errors by classroom or school has little impact on the standard errors in the regression speciÖcation in, for example,

31:1044:84550:0:21:88:539:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Column 5 of Table VII restricts the sample to students assigned to large classes, to test for class

31:1057:85754:21:20:88:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:TheFtests in Table VII rely on parametric assumptions to test the null of no class e§ects.
31:1058:85844:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:As a robustness check, we run permutation tests in which we randomly permute students between
31:1059:85937:20:21:72:539:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classes within each school. For each random permutation, we calculate theFstatistic on the class
31:1060:86033:21:20:71:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:dummies.  Using the empirical distribution ofFstatistics from 1,000 within-school permutations
31:1061:86127:20:20:71:539:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of students, we calculate a non-parametricpvalue based on where the trueFstatistic (from row
31:1062:86219:20:21:71:539:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1) falls in the empirical distribution. Reassuringly, these non-parametricpvalues are quite similar
31:1063:86318:21:29:71:493:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to those produced from the parametricFtest, as shown in the second row of Table VII.
31:1064:86402:29:27:71:401:293:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:V.C.  Covariance between Class E§ects on Scores and Earnings
31:1065:86462:27:21:71:539:266:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Having established class e§ects on both scores and earnings, we estimate the covariance of these
31:1066:86558:21:31:71:328:245:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:class e§ects using regression speciÖcations of the form
31:1067:86613:31:31:71:402:214:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(12)yicnw=-nw+\f-s-icnw+Xicnw-+"icnw,
31:1068:86650:31:20:71:540:183:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:whereyicnwrepresents an outcome for studentiwho enters classcin schoolnin entry grade (wave)
31:1069:86742:20:21:71:540:163:11:g_d0_f4:11:g_d0_f4:w.  The regressor of interest-s-icnwis our leave-out mean measure of peer test scores for studenti
31:1070:86840:21:20:71:539:142:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:at the end of entry gradew, as deÖned in equation (9).31In the baseline speciÖcations, we include
31:1071:86937:20:20:71:539:122:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:students in all entry grades to analyze how the quality of the studentís randomly assigned Örst
31:1072:87032:20:7:71:470:102:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:correlation coe¢ cient is small.  We thank Gary Chamberlain for helpful comments on these issues.
31:1073:87129:7:15:80:542:95:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:31Sacerdote (2001) employs analagous regression speciÖcations to detect clustering in randomly assigned roommatesí
31:1074:87243:15:0:71:147:80:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:ex-post test scores.
32:1075:87265:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:class a§ects long-term outcomes. We then test for di§erences in the impacts of class quality across
32:1076:87364:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:grades K-3 by estimating equation (12) for separate entry grades.  As above, we cluster standard
32:1077:87460:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:errors at the school level to adjust for the fact that outcomes are correlated across students within
32:1078:87561:20:21:72:260:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classrooms and possibly within schools.
32:1079:87600:21:20:88:539:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We begin by characterizing the impact of class quality on test scores.  Figure IVa plots each
32:1080:87693:20:20:72:543:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:studentís end-of-grade test scores vs. his entry-grade class quality, as measured by his classmatesí
32:1081:87793:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:test scores minus his schoolmatesítest scores.  The graph adjusts for school-by-entry-grade e§ects
32:1082:87891:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to isolate the random variation in class quality using the technique in Figure IIIa; it does not
32:1083:87987:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:adjust for parent and student controls.   Figure IVa shows that children randomly assigned to
32:1084:88080:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:higher quality classes upon entry ñi.e., classes where their peers score higher on the end of year
32:1085:88178:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:test ñhave higher test scores at the end of the year.  A one percentile increase in entry-year class
32:1086:88278:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:quality is estimated to raise own test scores by 0.68 percentiles, conÖrming that test scores are
32:1087:88375:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:highly correlated across students within a classroom.  Figure IVb replicates Figure IVa, changing
32:1088:88472:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the dependent variable to 8th grade test score.  Consistent with the earlier ANOVA results, the
32:1089:88567:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impact fades out by grade 8.  A one percentile increase in the quality of the studentís entry-year
32:1090:88665:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classroom raises 8th grade test scores by only 0.08 percentiles. Figure IVc uses the same design to
32:1091:88764:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:evaluate the e§ects of class quality on adult wage earnings.  Students assigned to a one percentile
32:1092:88863:20:20:72:463:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:higher quality class have $56.6 (0.4%) higher earnings on average over ages 25-27.
32:1093:88945:20:21:88:539:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We verify that our method of measuring class quality does not generate a mechanical correlation
32:1094:89040:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:between peers scores and own outcomes using permutation tests.  We randomly permute students
32:1095:89132:20:20:72:539:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:across classrooms within schools and replicate equation (12).  We use thetstatistics on\ffrom
32:1096:89225:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the random permutations to form an empirical cdf oftstatistics under the null hypothesis of no
32:1097:89319:21:20:72:539:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:class e§ects.  We Önd that fewer than 0.001% of thetstatistics from the random permutations
32:1098:89410:20:20:72:539:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:are larger than the actualtstatistic on kindergarten test score in Figure IVa of 22.7.  For the
32:1099:89505:20:21:72:539:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings outcome, fewer than 0.1% of thetstatistics from the random permutations are larger
32:1100:89596:21:20:72:539:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f4:than the actualtstatistic of 3.55.  These non-parametric permutation tests conÖrm that thep
32:1101:89687:20:20:72:416:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:values obtained using parametric t-tests are accurate in our application.
32:1102:89760:20:21:88:539:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:As noted above, part of the relationship between earnings and peersítest scores may be driven
32:1103:89853:21:20:72:540:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by reáection bias: high ability students raise their peersíscores and themselves have high earnings.
32:1104:89953:20:20:72:540:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:This could generate a correlation between peer scores and own earnings even if class quality has no
32:1105:90052:20:20:72:540:99:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:causal impact on earnings.  However, the fact that end-of-kindergarten peer scores are not highly
32:1106:90149:20:0:72:540:79:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:correlated with 8th grade test scores (Figure IVb) places a tight upper bound on the degree of this
33:1107:90250:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:bias. In the presence of reáection bias, a high ability student (who raises her classroom peersíscores
33:1108:90352:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in the year she enters) should also score highly on 8th grade tests, creating a spurious correlation
33:1109:90452:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:between Örst-classroom peer scores and own 8th grade scores.  Therefore, if Örst-classroom peer
33:1110:90547:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:scores have zero correlation with 8th grade scores, there cannot be any reáection bias.  In Online
33:1111:90645:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Appendix B, we formalize this argument by deriving a bound on the degree of reáection bias in a
33:1112:90740:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:linear-in-means model as a function of the empirical estimates in Table VIII and the cross-sectional
33:1113:90840:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:correlations between test scores and earnings.  If class quality has no causal impact on earnings
33:1114:90937:21:20:72:539:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(\f= 0), the upper bound on the regression coe¢ cient of earnings on class quality is $9, less than
33:1115:91036:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:20% of our empirical estimate of $56.6. Although this quantitative bound relies on the parametric
33:1116:91133:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:assumptions of a linear-in-means model, it captures a more general intuition: the rapid fade out
33:1117:91229:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of class quality e§ects on test scores rules out signiÖcant reáection bias in impacts of peer scores
33:1118:91329:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on later adult outcomes.   Recall that the class quality estimates also su§er from a downward
33:1119:91422:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:attenuation bias of 23%, the same magnitude as the upper bound on the reáection bias.   We
33:1120:91512:21:20:72:479:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:therefore proceed by using end-of-year peer scores as a simple proxy for class quality.
33:1121:91599:20:20:88:539:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Figure Va characterizes the time path of the impact of class quality on earnings, dividing
33:1122:91689:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classrooms in two groups ñ those with class quality above and below the median.   The time
33:1123:91779:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:pattern of the total class quality impact is similar to the impact of teacher experience shown in
33:1124:91876:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Figure IIIc. Prior to 2004, there is little di§erence in earnings between the two curves, but the gap
33:1125:91977:20:21:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:noticeably widens beginning in 2003.  By 2007, students who were assigned to classes of above-
33:1126:92071:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:median quality are earning $875 (5.5%) more on average.  Figure Vb shows the time path of the
33:1127:92164:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impacts on college attendance.  Students in higher quality classes are more likely to be attending
33:1128:92262:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:college in their early 20ís, consistent with their higher earnings and steeper earnings trajectories in
33:1129:92365:21:20:72:125:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:later years.

34:1144:93687:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Recall from Table V that students assigned to small classes scored 4.8 percentile points higher on
