34:1150:94189:20:20:88:539:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Column 3 of Table VIII isolates the variation in class quality that is orthogonal to observable

34:1153:94484:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:conditional on these observables, conÖrming that components of class quality orthogonal to observ-
34:1154:94582:20:21:72:194:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ables matter for earnings.
34:1155:94608:21:20:88:539:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The preceding speciÖcations pool grades K-3.  Column 4 restricts the sample to kindergarten

35:1172:96158:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:point estimates of the impacts are positive for all of the outcomes.  Online Appendix Table XI

35:1175:96419:20:21:88:539:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Finally, we check the robustness of our results by implementing instrumental-variable methods
35:1176:96512:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of detecting covariance between class e§ects on scores and earnings. The e§ects of class quality on

35:1178:96707:20:21:72:539:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:IV estimate of the earnings gain associated with an increase in test scores:$50:61=0:662 = $76:48.
35:1179:96805:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:That is, class-level factors that raise test scores by one percentile point raise earnings by $76.48
35:1180:96905:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on average.  In Online Appendix Table XIII, we show that other IV estimators yield very similar
35:1181:97000:20:21:72:119:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:estimates.
35:1182:97010:21:20:88:539:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:While class e§ects on scores and earnings are highly correlated, a substantial portion of class
35:1183:97105:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:e§ects on earnings is orthogonal to our measure of class quality. Using a random e§ects estimator
35:1184:97202:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:as in Column 4 of Table VII, we Önd that the standard deviation of class e§ects on earnings falls
35:1185:97299:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:from $1520 to $1372 after we control for our peer-score class quality measure-s-icnw.   Hence,
35:1186:97393:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:roughly1-(13721520)2-1=5of the variance of the class e§ect on earnings comes through class e§ects
35:1187:97490:20:35:72:139:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on test scores.
35:1188:97505:35:31:131:481:369:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:VI.  Fade-Out, Re-Emergence, and Non-Cognitive Skills
35:1189:97558:31:20:72:540:338:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:In this section, we explore why the impacts of class size and class quality in early childhood fade
35:1190:97657:20:20:72:540:318:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:out on tests administered in later grades but re-emerge in adulthood.  In order to have a Öxed
35:1191:97751:20:21:72:540:298:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:benchmark to document fade-out, we use only kindergarten entrants throughout this section and
35:1192:97844:21:20:72:503:277:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:analyze the impacts of KG class quality on test scores and other outcomes in later grades.
35:1193:97934:20:20:88:539:257:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We Örst document the fade-out e§ect using the class quality measure by estimating equation
35:1194:98024:20:21:72:540:237:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(12) with test scores in each grade as the dependent variable and with the standard vector of parent
35:1195:98124:21:20:72:540:216:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and student controls as well as school Öxed e§ects.  Figure VIa plots the estimated impacts on
35:1196:98218:20:20:72:540:196:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:test scores in grades K-8 of increasing KG class quality by one (within-school) standard deviation.
35:1197:98317:20:21:72:540:176:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:A one (within school) SD increase in KG class quality increases end-of-kindergarten test scores by
35:1198:98415:21:20:72:540:155:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:6.27 percentiles, consistent with our Öndings above. In grade 1, students who were in a 1 SD better
35:1199:98514:20:20:72:540:135:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:KG class score approximately 1.50 percentile points higher on end-of-year tests, an e§ect that is
35:1200:98611:20:21:72:539:115:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:signiÖcant withp <0:001. The e§ect gradually fades over time, and by grade 4 students who were
35:1201:98705:21:16:71:389:94:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:in a better KG class no longer score signiÖcantly higher on tests.34
35:1202:98773:16:0:80:539:78:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:34This fade-out e§ect is consistent with the rapid fade-out of teacher e§ects documented by Jacob, Lefgren, and
36:1203:98886:0:21:88:539:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:If a one percentile increase in 8th grade test scores is more valuable than a one percentile
36:1204:98978:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:increase in KG test scores, then the evidence in Figure VIa would not necessarily imply that the
36:1205:99074:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:e§ects of early childhood education fade out. To evaluate this possibility, we convert the test score
36:1206:99175:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impacts to predicted earnings gains.  We run separate OLS regressions of earnings on the test
36:1207:99268:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:scores for each grade from K-8 to estimate the cross-sectional relationship between each gradeís
36:1208:99364:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:test score and earnings (see Online Appendix Table V, Column 1 for these coe¢ cients).  We then
36:1209:99459:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:multiply the class quality e§ect on scores shown in Figure VIa by the corresponding coe¢ cient on
36:1210:99556:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:scores from the OLS earnings regression.  Figure VIb plots the earnings impacts predicted by the
36:1211:99652:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:test score gains in each grade that arise from attending a better KG class.  The pattern in Figure
36:1212:99750:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:VIb looks very similar to that in Figure VIa, showing that there is indeed substantial fade-out of
36:1213:99848:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the KG class quality e§ect on predicted earnings. By 4th grade, one would predict less than a $50
36:1214:99945:20:20:72:487:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:per year gain in earnings from a better KG class based on observed test score impacts.
36:1215:100031:20:21:88:539:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The Önal point in Figure VIb shows the actual observed earnings impact of a one SD improve-
36:1216:100122:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:ment in KG class quality.  The actual impact of $483 is similar to what one would have predicted
36:1217:100218:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:based on the improvement in KG test scores ($588).  The impacts of early childhood education
36:1218:100310:20:21:72:416:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:re-emerge in adulthood despite fading out on test scores in later grades.
36:1219:100383:21:20:88:539:383:11:g_d0_f5:11:g_d0_f2:Non-Cognitive Skills.One potential explanation for fade-out and re-emergence is the acquisition
36:1220:100478:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of non-cognitive skills (e.g.  Heckman 2000, Heckman, Stixrud, and Urzua 2006, Lindqvist and
36:1221:100570:20:21:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Vestman 2011). We evaluate whether non-cognitive skills could explain our Öndings using data on
36:1222:100665:21:20:72:472:322:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:non-cognitive measures collected for a subset of STAR students in grades 4 and 8.35
36:1223:100748:20:20:88:539:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Finn et al. (2007) and Dee and West (2008) describe the non-cognitive measures in the STAR
36:1224:100838:20:21:71:539:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:data in detail; we provide a brief summary here. In grade 4, teachers in the STAR schools were asked
36:1225:100938:21:20:71:539:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to evaluate a random subset of their students on a scale of 1-5 on several behavioral measures, such
36:1226:101038:20:20:71:539:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:as whether the student ìannoys others.î These responses were consolidated into four standardized
36:1227:101134:20:21:71:539:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:scales measuring each studentís e§ort, initiative, nonparticipatory behavior, and how the student
36:1228:101231:21:20:71:539:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is seen to ìvalueî the class.  In grade 8, math and English teachers were asked to rate a subset
36:1229:101327:20:20:71:539:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of their students on a similar set of questions, which were again consolidated into the same four
36:1230:101424:20:21:71:539:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:standardized scales.  To obtain a measure analogous to our percentile measure of test scores, we
36:1231:101520:21:19:71:539:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:construct percentile measures for these four scales and compute the average percentile score for
36:1232:101616:19:7:71:274:120:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:Sims (2008), Kane and Staiger (2008), and others.
36:1233:101665:7:15:80:539:113:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:35Previous studies have used the STAR data to investigate whether class size a§ects non-cognitive skills (Finn et al.
36:1234:101782:15:11:71:539:98:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:1989, Dee and West 2008). They Önd mixed evidence on the impact of class size on non-cognitive skills: statistically
36:1235:101898:11:11:71:539:87:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:signiÖcant impacts are detected in grade 4, but not in grade 8.  Here, we analyze the impacts of our broader class
36:1236:102012:11:0:71:137:76:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:quality measure.
37:1237:102030:0:21:72:531:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:each student.  For 8th grade, we then take the average of the math and English teacher ratings.
37:1238:102125:21:20:88:539:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Among the 6,025 students who entered Project STAR in KG and whom we match in the IRS
37:1239:102209:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:data, we have data on non-cognitive skills for 1,671 (28%) in grade 4 and 1,780 (30%) in grade
37:1240:102303:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:8.   The availability of non-cognitive measures for only a subset of the students who could be
37:1241:102397:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tracked until grade 8 naturally raises concerns about selective attrition.  Dee and West (2008)
37:1242:102492:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:investigate this issue in detail, and we replicate their Öndings with our expanded set of parental
37:1243:102590:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:characteristics.  In grade 8, we Önd no signiÖcant di§erences in the probability of having non-
37:1244:102685:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:cognitive data by KG classrooms or class types (small vs. large), and conÖrm that in this sample
37:1245:102781:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the observable background characteristics are balanced across classrooms and class types.   In
37:1246:102875:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:grade 4, non-cognitive data are signiÖcantly more likely to be available for students assigned to
37:1247:102972:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:small classes, but among the sample with non-cognitive data there are no signiÖcant di§erences in
37:1248:103069:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:background characteristics across classrooms or class types.  Hence, the sample for whom we have
37:1249:103165:20:21:72:538:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:non-cognitive data appear to be balanced across classrooms at least on observable characteristics.
37:1250:103263:21:20:88:539:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We begin by estimating the cross-sectional correlation between non-cognitive outcomes and
37:1251:103352:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings. Column 1 of Table IX shows that a 1 percentile improvement in non-cognitive measures in
37:1252:103449:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:grade 4 is associated with a $106 gain in earnings conditional on the standard vector of demographic
37:1253:103549:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:characteristics used above and school-by-entry-grade Öxed e§ects. Column 2 shows that controlling
37:1254:103646:20:20:72:540:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for math and reading test scores in grade 4 reduces the predictive power of non-cognitive scores
37:1255:103742:20:21:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:only slightly, to $88 per percentile.  In contrast, column 3 shows that non-cognitive skills in grade
37:1256:103843:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:4 are relatively weak predictors of 8th grade test scores when compared with math and reading
37:1257:103936:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:scores in 4th grade.  Because non-cognitive skills appear to be correlated with earnings through
37:1258:104032:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:channels that are not picked up by subsequent standardized tests, they could explain fade-out and
37:1259:104129:21:20:72:137:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:re-emergence.
37:1260:104142:20:20:88:539:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:To further evaluate this mechanism, we investigate the e§ects of KG class quality on non-
37:1261:104231:20:21:72:540:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:cognitive skills in grade 4 and 8.  As a reference, column 4 shows that a 1 percentile improvement
37:1262:104329:21:20:72:540:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in KG class quality increases a studentís test scores in grade 4 by a statistically insigniÖcant 0.05
37:1263:104430:20:20:72:540:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:percentiles. In contrast, column 5 shows that the same improvement in KG class quality generates
37:1264:104526:20:21:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:a statistically signiÖcant increase of 0.15 percentiles in the index of non-cognitive measures in grade
37:1265:104629:21:20:72:539:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:4. Columns 6 and 7 replicate columns 4 and 5 for grade 8.36Again, KG class quality does not have
37:1266:104725:20:16:72:540:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:a signiÖcant impact on 8th grade test scores but has a signiÖcant impact on non-cognitive measures.
37:1267:104824:16:15:80:539:103:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:36We use all KG entrants for whom test scores are available in columns 4 and 6 to increase precision.  The point
37:1268:104936:15:0:72:540:88:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:estimates on test score impacts are similar for the subsample of students for whom non-cognitive data are available.
38:1269:105054:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Finally, columns 8 and 9 show that the experience of the studentís teacher in kindergarten ñwhich
38:1270:105151:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:we showed above also impacts earnings ñhas a small and statistically insigniÖcant impact on test
38:1271:105247:20:20:72:514:668:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:scores but a substantially larger impact on non-cognitive measures in 8th grade (p= 0:07).37
38:1272:105339:20:21:88:539:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We can translate the impacts on non-cognitive skills into predicted impacts on earnings fol-
38:1273:105431:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:lowing the method in Figure VIb.  We regress earnings on the non-cognitive measure in grade 4,
38:1274:105525:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:conditioning on demographic characteristics, and obtain an OLS coe¢ cient of $101 per percentile.
38:1275:105622:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Multiplying this OLS coe¢ cient by the estimated impact of class quality on non-cognitive skills
38:1276:105718:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in grade 4, we predict that a 1 SD improvement in KG class quality will increase earnings by
38:1277:105810:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:$139.  The same exercise for 4th grade math+reading test scores yields a predicted earnings gain
38:1278:105906:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of $40.  These results suggest that improvements in non-cognitive skills explain a larger share
38:1279:106001:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of actual earnings gains than improvements in cognitive performance, consistent with Heckman
38:1280:106093:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:et al.ís (2010) Öndings for the Perry Preschool program.  In contrast, a one standard deviation
38:1281:106188:20:21:72:539:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:increase in class quality is predicted to raise 8th grade test scores by only0:47percentiles based on
38:1282:106289:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:its observed impacts on non-cognitive skills in grade 4 and the cross-sectional correlation between
38:1283:106388:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:grade 4 non-cognitive skills and grade 8 test scores.  This predicted impact is quite close to the
38:1284:106486:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:actual impact of class quality on 8th grade scores of0:57percentiles.  Hence, the impacts of class
38:1285:106584:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:quality on non-cognitive skills is consistent with both fade-out on scores and re-emergence on adult
38:1286:106684:20:20:72:119:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:outcomes.
38:1287:106693:20:21:88:539:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Intuitively, a better kindergarten classroom might simultaneously increase performance on end-
38:1288:106787:21:20:72:540:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of-year tests and improve untested non-cognitive skills.  For instance, a KG teacher who is able to
38:1289:106886:20:20:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:make her students memorize vocabulary words may instill social skills in the process of managing
38:1290:106982:20:21:72:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:her classroom successfully.  These non-cognitive skills may not be well measured by standardized
38:1291:107078:21:20:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:tests, leading to very rapid fade-out immediately after KG. However, these skills could still have
38:1292:107176:20:20:72:206:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:returns in the labor market.
38:1293:107204:20:21:88:539:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Although non-cognitive skills provide one plausible explanation of the data, our analysis is far
38:1294:107300:21:20:72:540:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:from deÖnitive proof of the importance of non-cognitive skills.  The estimates of non-cognitive
38:1295:107395:20:20:72:540:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impacts could su§er from attrition bias and are somewhat imprecisely estimated.  Moreover, our
38:1296:107489:20:21:72:540:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:analysis does not show that manipulating non-cognitive skills directly has causal impacts on adult
38:1297:107587:21:20:72:540:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:outcomes.  We have shown that high quality KG classes improve both non-cognitive skills and
38:1298:107678:20:16:72:540:119:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:adult outcomes, but the mechanism through which adult outcomes are improved could run through
38:1299:107771:16:15:80:539:103:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:37Online Appendix Table XIV decomposes the relationships described in Table IX into the four constituent com-
38:1300:107880:15:0:72:192:88:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:ponents of non-cognitive skill.
39:1301:107913:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:another channel that is correlated with the acquisition of non-cognitive skills. It would be valuable
39:1302:108014:21:34:72:450:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to analyze interventions that target non-cognitive skills directly in future work.
39:1303:108096:34:31:252:358:654:14:g_d0_f2:14:g_d0_f2:VII.  Conclusion
39:1304:108112:31:21:72:540:623:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The impacts of education have traditionally been measured by achievement on standardized tests.
39:1305:108207:21:20:72:540:602:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:This paper has shown that the classroom environments that raise test scores also improve long-term
39:1306:108305:20:20:72:540:582:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:outcomes.  Students who were randomly assigned to higher quality classrooms in grades K-3 earn
39:1307:108399:20:21:72:540:562:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:more, are more likely to attend college, save more for retirement, and live in better neighborhoods.
39:1308:108499:21:20:72:540:541:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Yet the same students do not do much better on standardized tests in later grades.  These results
39:1309:108596:20:20:72:540:521:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:suggest that policy makers may wish to rethink the objective of raising test scores and evaluating
39:1310:108694:20:21:72:540:501:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:interventions via long-term test score gains.  Researchers who had examined only the impacts of
39:1311:108789:21:20:72:540:480:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:STAR on test scores would have incorrectly concluded that early childhood education does not
39:1312:108881:20:20:72:540:460:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:have long-lasting impacts.  While the quality of education is best judged by directly measuring its
39:1313:108980:20:21:72:540:440:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impacts on adult outcomes, our analysis suggests thatcontemporaneous(end-of-year) test scores
39:1314:109073:21:20:71:409:419:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:are a reasonably good short-run measure of the quality of a classroom.
39:1315:109143:20:20:88:539:399:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We conclude by using our empirical estimates to provide rough calculations of the beneÖts of
39:1316:109235:20:20:71:539:379:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:various policy interventions (see Online Appendix C for details).  These cost-beneÖt calculations
39:1317:109332:20:21:71:539:359:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:rely on several strong assumptions.  We assume that the percentage gain in earnings observed at
39:1318:109427:21:20:71:539:338:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:age 27 remains constant over the lifecycle.  We ignore non-monetary returns to education (such as
39:1319:109524:20:20:71:539:318:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reduced crime) as well as general equilibrium e§ects.  We discount earnings gains at a 3% annual
39:1320:109620:20:21:71:302:298:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:rate back to age 6, the point of the intervention.
39:1321:109670:21:20:88:539:277:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(1) Class Quality. The random-e§ects estimate reported in column 4 of Table VII implies that
39:1322:109762:20:20:71:539:257:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:increasing class quality by one standard deviation of the distribution within schools raises earnings
39:1323:109863:20:21:71:539:237:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by $1,520 (9.6%) at age 27.  Under the preceding assumptions, this translates into a lifetime
39:1324:109956:21:20:71:539:216:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings gain of approximately $39,100 for the average individual.   For a classroom of twenty
39:1325:110050:20:20:71:539:196:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:students, this implies a present-value beneÖt of $782,000 for improving class quality for a single
39:1326:110148:20:21:71:539:176:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:year by one (within-school) standard deviation.  This large Ögure includes all potential beneÖts
39:1327:110244:21:20:71:539:155:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:from an improved classroom environment, including better peers, teachers, and random shocks,
39:1328:110336:20:20:71:539:135:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and hence is useful primarily for understanding the stakes at play in early childhood education. It
39:1329:110435:20:21:71:539:115:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:is less helpful from a policy perspective because one cannot implement interventions that directly
39:1330:110533:21:20:71:539:94:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:improve classroom quality. This motivates the analysis of class size and better teachers, two factors
39:1331:110634:20:0:71:246:74:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:that contribute to classroom quality.
40:1332:110673:0:21:88:539:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(2) Class Size.  We calculate the beneÖts of reducing class size by 33% in two ways.  The Örst
40:1333:110767:21:20:72:540:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:method uses the estimated earnings gain from being assigned to a small class reported in column
40:1334:110862:20:20:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:5 of Table V.  The point estimate of $4 in Table V translates into a lifetime earnings gain from
40:1335:110958:20:21:72:540:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reducing class size by 33% for one year of $103 in present value per student, or $2,057 for a class that
40:1336:111062:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:originally had twenty students. But this estimate is imprecise: the 95% conÖdence interval for the
40:1337:111160:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:lifetime earnings gain of reducing class size by 33% for one year ranges from -$17,500 to $17,700 per
40:1338:111261:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:child. To obtain more precision, we predict the beneÖts of class size reduction using the estimated
40:1339:111360:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:impact of classroom quality on scores and earnings.  We estimate that a 1 percentile increase in
40:1340:111456:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:class quality raises test scores by 0.66 percentiles and earnings by $50.6, implying an earnings gain
40:1341:111557:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of $76.7 per percentile increase in test scores. Next, we make the strong assumption that the ratio
40:1342:111656:21:20:72:540:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of earnings gains to test score gains is the same for changes in class size as it is for improvements
40:1343:111757:20:20:72:540:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in class quality more generally.  Under this assumption, a 33% class size reduction in grades K-3
40:1344:111854:20:21:72:539:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:(which raised test scores by 4.8 percentiles) is predicted to raise earnings by4:8-$76:7 = $368
40:1345:111949:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(2.3%) at age 27.  This calculation implies a present value earnings gain from class size reduction
40:1346:112048:20:20:72:335:424:11:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:of $9,460 per student and $189,000 for the classroom.38
40:1347:112103:20:21:88:539:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(3) Teachers. We cannot directly estimate the total impacts of teachers on earnings in this study
40:1348:112200:21:20:71:539:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:because we observe each teacher in only one classroom, making it impossible to separate teacher
40:1349:112295:20:20:71:539:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:e§ects from peer e§ects and classroom-level shocks.  However, we can predict the magnitudes of
40:1350:112389:20:21:71:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:teacher e§ects as measured by value-added on test scores by drawing upon prior work.  Rocko§
40:1351:112481:21:20:71:539:322:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(2004), Rivkin, Hanushek, and Kain (2005), and Kane and Staiger (2008) use datasets with multiple
40:1352:112578:20:20:71:539:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:classrooms per teacher to estimate that a one standard deviation increase in teacher quality raises
40:1353:112677:20:21:71:540:282:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:test scores by between 0.1 and 0.2 standard deviations (2.7-5.4 percentiles).39Under the strong
40:1354:112772:21:20:71:539:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:assumption that the ratio of earnings gains to test score gains is the same for changes in teacher
40:1355:112870:20:20:71:540:241:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f3:quality and class quality more broadly, this test score gain implies an earnings gain of$208-$416
40:1356:112967:20:21:71:539:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(1.3%-2.6%) at age 27 and a present-value earnings gain ranging from $5,350-$10,700 per student.
40:1357:113063:21:20:71:539:200:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Hence, we predict that a one standard deviation improvement in teacher quality in a single year
40:1358:113158:20:20:71:539:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:would generate earnings gains between $107,000 and $214,000 for a classroom of twenty students.
40:1359:113253:20:17:71:539:160:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:These predictions are roughly consistent with the Öndings of Chetty, Friedman, and Rocko§ (2011),
40:1360:113350:17:15:80:539:143:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:38Krueger (1999) projects a gain from small-class attendence of$9;603for men and$7;851for women.  Neither
40:1361:113455:15:7:72:359:128:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:of our estimates are statistically distinguishable from these predictions.
40:1362:113529:7:14:80:539:121:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:39We use estimates of the impacts of teacher quality on scores from other studies to predict earnings gains because
40:1363:113644:14:11:72:540:107:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:we do not have repeat observations on teachers in our data.  In future work, it would be extremely valuable to
40:1364:113754:11:11:72:542:96:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:link datasets with repeat observations on teachers to administrative data on students in order to measure teachersí
40:1365:113869:11:0:72:188:85:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:impacts on earnings directly.
41:1366:113900:0:21:72:540:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:who directly estimate the impacts of teacher value-added on earnings using a dataset that contains
41:1367:113998:21:20:72:300:688:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:information on multiple classrooms per teacher.
41:1368:114045:20:20:88:539:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Our results suggest that good teachers could potentially create great social value, perhaps
41:1369:114136:20:21:72:539:648:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:several times larger than current teacher salaries.40However, our Öndings do not have direct
41:1370:114228:21:20:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:implications for optimal teacher salaries or merit pay policies as we do not know whether higher
41:1371:114324:20:20:72:540:607:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:salaries or merit pay would improve teacher quality.41Relative to e§orts that seek to improve the
41:1372:114421:20:21:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:quality of teachers, class size reductions have the important advantage of being more well-deÖned
41:1373:114518:21:20:72:540:566:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and straightforward to implement. However, reductions in class size must be implemented carefully
41:1374:114615:20:20:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to generate improvements in outcomes.  If schools are forced to reduce teacher and class quality
41:1375:114711:20:21:72:540:526:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:along other dimensions when reducing class size, the net gains from class size reduction may be
41:1376:114806:21:20:72:307:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:diminished (Jepsen and Rivkin 2009, Sims 2009).
41:1377:114853:20:20:88:539:485:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Finally, our analysis raises the possibility that di§erences in school quality perpetuate income
41:1378:114949:20:21:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:inequality.  In the U.S., higher income families have access to better public schools on average
41:1379:115045:21:20:72:540:444:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:because of property-tax Önance.   Using the class quality impacts reported above, Chetty and
41:1380:115137:20:20:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Friedman (2011) estimate that the intergenerational correlation of income would fall by roughly
41:1381:115232:20:21:72:540:404:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1/3 if all children attended schools of the same quality.  Improving early childhood education in
41:1382:115329:21:20:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:disadvantaged areas ñ e.g. through federal tax credits or tax policy reforms ñ could potentially
41:1383:115425:20:16:72:232:363:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reduce inequality in the long run.
41:1384:115459:16:15:80:539:347:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:40According to calculations from the 2006-2008 American Community Survey, the mean salary for elementary and
41:1385:115567:15:7:72:332:332:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:middle school teachers in the U.S. was $39,164 (in 2009 dollars).
41:1386:115632:7:15:80:539:325:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:41An analogy with executive compensation might be helpful in understanding this point.  CEOsídecisions have
41:1387:115739:15:11:72:540:310:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:large impacts on the Örms they run, and hence can create or destroy large amounts of economic value.  But this does
41:1388:115854:11:0:72:523:299:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:not necessarily imply that increasing CEO compensation or pay-for-performance would improve CEO decisions.
42:1389:115962:0:26:280:330:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:References

44:1492:123159:14:0:72:165:395:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:of Education, 1990.
45:1493:123180:0:26:72:461:709:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:Appendix A: Algorithm for Matching STAR Records to Tax Data
45:1494:123239:26:13:88:539:683:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The tax data were accessed through contract TIRNO-09-R-00007 with the Statistics of Income
45:1495:123329:13:14:72:540:670:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(SOI) Division at the US Internal Revenue Service. Requests for research contracts by SOI are
45:1496:123422:14:13:72:540:656:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:posted online at the Federal Business Opportunities https://www.fbo.gov/.  SOI also welcomes
45:1497:123514:13:14:72:460:643:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:research partnerships between outside academics and internal researchers at SOI.
45:1498:123594:14:13:88:539:629:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:STAR records were matched to tax data using social security number (SSN), date of birth,
45:1499:123682:13:14:72:540:616:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:gender, name, and STAR elementary school ZIP code. Note that STAR records do not contain all
45:1500:123774:14:13:72:540:602:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the same information. Almost every STAR record contains date of birth, gender, and last name.
45:1501:123867:13:14:72:540:589:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Some records contain no SSN while others contain multiple possible SSNs. Some records contain
45:1502:123960:14:13:72:441:575:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:no Örst name. A missing Öeld yielded a non-match unless otherwise speciÖed.
45:1503:124035:13:14:88:539:562:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We Örst discuss the general logic of the match algorithm and then document the routines in
45:1504:124125:14:14:72:540:548:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:detail.  The match algorithm was designed to match as many records as possible using variables
45:1505:124219:14:13:72:539:534:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:that arenotcontingent on ex post outcomes. SSN, date of birth, gender, and last name in the tax
45:1506:124314:13:14:72:540:521:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:data are populated by the Social Security Administration using information that is not contingent
45:1507:124411:14:13:72:540:507:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on ex post outcomes. First name and ZIP code in tax data are contingent on observing some ex
45:1508:124503:13:14:72:540:494:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:post outcome. First name data derive from information returns, which are typically generated after
45:1509:124601:14:13:72:540:480:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:an adult outcome like employment (W-2 forms), college attendance (1098-T forms), and mortgage
45:1510:124694:13:14:72:540:467:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:interest payment (1098 forms). The ZIP code on the claiming parentís 1040 return is typically from
45:1511:124792:14:13:72:540:453:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1996 and is thus contingent on the ex post outcome of the STAR subject not having moved far
45:1512:124883:13:14:72:254:440:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:from her elementary school by age 16.
45:1513:124920:14:13:88:539:426:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:89.8% of STAR records were matched using only ex ante information.  The algorithm Örst
45:1514:125006:13:14:72:540:413:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:matched as many records as possible using only SSN, date of birth, gender, and last name. It then
45:1515:125103:14:14:72:540:399:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:used Örst name only toexcludecandidate matches based on date of birth, gender, and last name,
45:1516:125196:14:13:72:540:385:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:often leaving only one candidate record remaining. Because that exclusion did not condition on an
45:1517:125293:13:14:72:540:372:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:information return having been Öled on behalf of that remaining candidate, these matches also did
45:1518:125390:14:13:72:240:358:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:not condition on ex post outcomes.
45:1519:125424:13:14:88:539:345:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The match algorithm proceeded as follows, generating seven match types denoted A through
45:1520:125512:14:13:72:540:331:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:G.  The matches generated purely through ex-ante information are denoted A through E below
45:1521:125602:13:14:72:540:318:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:and account for 89.8% of STAR records. Matches based on ex-post-information are denoted F and
45:1522:125695:14:13:72:540:304:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:G below and constitute an additional 5.4% of STAR records. The paper reports results using the
45:1523:125789:13:14:72:540:291:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:full 95.0% matched sample, but all the qualitative results hold in the 89.8% sample matched using
45:1524:125886:14:25:72:193:277:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:only ex ante information.
45:1525:125911:25:13:85:540:252:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:1. Match STAR records to tax records by SSN. For STAR records with multiple possible SSNs,
45:1526:126001:13:14:99:540:239:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:match on all of these SSNs to obtain a set of candidate tax record matches for each STAR
45:1527:126089:14:13:99:540:225:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:record with SSN information. Each candidate tax record contains date of birth, gender, and
45:1528:126179:13:26:99:390:212:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Örst four letters of every last name ever assigned to the SSN.
45:1529:126241:26:13:112:540:186:11:g_d0_f9:11:g_d0_f2:-Match Type A. Keep unique matches after matching on Örst four letters of last name,
45:1530:126325:13:17:123:246:173:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:date of birth, and gender.

50:1846:136509:30:13:75:540:493:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:For earnings, we estimate-e= $90:04 (SE= $8:65)in Table IV. Hence, if the entire e§ect of class
50:1847:136604:13:27:72:377:480:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:quality on earnings were due to reáection bias, we would obtain
50:1848:136667:27:10:179:241:453:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f4:^bLMe=-e-
50:1849:136676:10:0:215:251:443:11:g_d0_f3:8:g_d0_f7:(1 +-)2
50:1850:136683:0:30:256:432:451:11:g_d0_f3:11:g_d0_f3:= $90:04-0955 = $8:60 (SE= $5:49)
50:1851:136716:30:14:72:540:421:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:where the standard error is computed using the delta method under the assumption that the
50:1852:136805:14:13:72:540:407:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:estimates of^bLMs,-s, and-eare uncorrelated.  This upper bound of$8:60due to reáection bias is
50:1853:136899:13:14:72:539:394:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:only 17% of the estimate of^bLMe= $50:61 (SE= $17:45)in Table VIII. Note that the degree of
50:1854:136990:14:13:72:540:380:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reáection bias would be smaller in the presence of class quality e§ects (\f >0); hence, 17% is an
50:1855:137087:13:26:72:486:367:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:upper bound on the degree of reáection bias in a linear-in-means model of peer e§ects.
50:1856:137173:26:25:72:279:341:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:Appendix C: Cost-BeneÖt Analysis
50:1857:137205:25:14:88:539:316:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We make the following assumptions to calculate the beneÖts of the policies considered in the
50:1858:137297:14:14:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:conclusion.  First, following Krueger (1999), we assume a 3% annual discount rate and discount
50:1859:137391:14:13:72:540:288:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:all earnings streams back to age 6, the point of the intervention.  Second, we use the mean wage
50:1860:137487:13:14:72:540:275:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings of a random sample of the U.S. population in 2007 as a baseline earnings proÖle over the
50:1861:137584:14:13:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:lifecycle.  Third, because we can observe earnings impacts only up to age 27, we must make an
50:1862:137677:13:14:72:540:248:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:assumption about the impacts after that point.  We assume that the percentage gain observed
50:1863:137768:14:13:72:540:234:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:at age 27 remains constant over the lifecycle.  This assumption may understate the total beneÖts
50:1864:137864:13:14:72:540:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:because the earnings impacts appear to grow over time, for example as college graduates have
50:1865:137956:14:13:72:540:207:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:steeper earnings proÖles.  Finally, our calculations ignore non-monetary returns to education such
50:1866:138054:13:14:72:540:194:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:as reduced crime.  They also ignore general equilibrium e§ects: increasing the education of the
50:1867:138149:14:13:72:540:180:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:population at large would increase the supply of skilled labor and may depress wage rates for more
50:1868:138247:13:14:72:540:167:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:educated individuals, reducing total social beneÖts.  Under these assumptions, we calculate the
50:1869:138342:14:14:72:540:153:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:present-value earnings gains for a classroom of 20 students from three interventions: improvements
50:1870:138440:14:13:72:465:139:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in classroom quality, reductions in class size, and improvements in teacher quality.
50:1871:138524:13:14:88:539:126:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(1) Class Quality. The random-e§ects estimate reported in column 4 of Table VII implies that
50:1872:138616:14:13:72:540:112:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:increasing class quality by one standard deviation of the distribution within schools raises earnings
50:1873:138717:13:14:72:540:99:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:by $1,520 (9.6%) at age 27.   Under the preceding assumptions, this translates into a lifetime
50:1874:138811:14:13:72:540:85:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings gain of approximately $39,100 for the average individual.  This implies a present-value
50:1875:138907:13:0:72:490:72:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:beneÖt of $782,000 for improving class quality by one within-school standard deviation.
51:1876:138996:0:14:88:539:709:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(2) Class Size.  We calculate the beneÖts of reducing class size by 33% in two ways.  The Örst
51:1877:139090:14:14:72:540:695:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:method uses the estimated earnings gain from being assigned to a small class reported in column
51:1878:139185:14:13:72:540:681:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:5 of Table V.  The point estimate of $4 in Table V translates into a lifetime earnings gain from
51:1879:139281:13:14:72:540:668:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reducing class size by 33% for one year of $103 in present value per student, or $2,057 for a class
51:1880:139380:14:13:72:540:654:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:that originally had twenty students.  But this estimate is imprecise: the 95% conÖdence interval
51:1881:139476:13:14:72:540:641:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for the lifetime earnings gain of reducing class size by 33% for one year ranges from -$17,500 to
51:1882:139573:14:13:72:540:627:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:$17,700 per child. Moreover, the results for other measures such as college attendance suggest that
51:1883:139672:13:14:72:314:614:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:the earnings impact may be larger in the long run.
51:1884:139722:14:13:88:539:600:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:To obtain more precise estimates, we predict the gains from class size reduction using the
51:1885:139812:13:14:72:540:587:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:estimated impact of classroom quality on scores and earnings.  We estimate that a 1 percentile
51:1886:139906:14:13:72:540:573:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:increase in class quality raises test scores by 0.66 percentiles and earnings by $50.6.  This implies
51:1887:140007:13:14:72:540:560:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:an earnings gain of $76.67 per percentile (or 13.1% per standard deviation) increase in test scores.
51:1888:140107:14:14:72:540:546:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:We make the strong assumption that the ratio of earnings gains to test score gains is the same
51:1889:140201:14:13:72:539:532:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:for changes in class size as it is for improvements in class quality more generally.44Under this
51:1890:140297:13:14:72:540:519:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:assumption, smaller classes (which raised test scores by 4.8 percentiles) are predicted to raise
51:1891:140393:14:13:72:539:505:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings by4:8-$76:7 = $368(2.3%) at age 27. This calculation implies a present value earnings
51:1892:140487:13:14:72:472:492:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:gain from class size reduction of $9,460 per student and $189,000 for the classroom.
51:1893:140571:14:13:88:539:478:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:Calculations analogous to those in Krueger (1999) imply that the average cost per child of
51:1894:140661:13:14:72:540:465:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:reducing class size by 33% for 2.14 years (the mean treatment duration for STAR students) is
51:1895:140753:14:13:72:539:451:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:$9,355 in 2009 dollars.45Our second calculation suggests that the beneÖt of reducing class size
51:1896:140848:13:14:72:540:438:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:might outweigh the costs. However, we must wait for more time to elapse before we can determine
51:1897:140943:14:14:72:540:424:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:whether the predicted earnings gains based on the class quality estimates are in fact realized by
51:1898:141040:14:13:72:241:410:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:those who attended smaller classes.
51:1899:141075:13:14:88:539:397:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(3) Teachers.  We calculate the beneÖts of improving teacher quality in two ways.  The Örst
51:1900:141166:14:13:72:540:383:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:method uses the estimated earnings gain of $57 from being assigned to a kindergarten teacher with
51:1901:141263:13:14:72:540:370:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:one year of extra experience, reported in Figure IIIb. The standard deviation of teacher experience
51:1902:141362:14:13:72:540:356:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:in our sample is 5.8 years.  Hence, a one standard deviation increase in teacher experience raises
51:1903:141460:13:14:72:540:343:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings by $331 (2.1%) at age 27. This translates into a lifetime earnings gain of $8,500 in present
51:1904:141561:14:13:72:303:329:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:value, or $170,000 for a class of twenty students.
51:1905:141611:13:14:88:539:316:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:The limitation of the preceding calculation is that it is based upon only one observable aspect of
51:1906:141709:14:13:72:540:302:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:teacher quality. To incorporate other aspects of teacher quality, we again develop a prediction based
51:1907:141810:13:14:72:540:289:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:on the impacts of class quality on scores and earnings. Rocko§ (2004), Rivkin, Hanushek, and Kain
51:1908:141907:14:14:72:540:275:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:(2005), and Kane and Staiger (2008) use datasets with repeated teacher observations to estimate
51:1909:142002:14:13:72:540:261:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:that a one standard deviation increase in teacher quality raises test scores by approximately 0.2
51:1910:142099:13:14:72:540:248:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:standard deviations (5.4 percentiles). Under the strong assumption that the ratio of earnings gains
51:1911:142198:14:13:72:540:234:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:to test score gains is the same for changes in teacher quality and class quality more broadly, this
51:1912:142297:13:14:72:539:221:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:translates into an earnings gain of5:4-$76:7 = $416(2.6%) at age 27. This implies a present-value
51:1913:142394:14:13:72:540:207:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:earnings gain of $10,700 per student. A one standard deviation improvement in teacher quality in
51:1914:142490:13:16:72:453:194:11:g_d0_f2:11:g_d0_f2:a single year generates earnings gains of $214,000 for a class of twenty students.
51:1915:142572:16:15:80:539:178:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:44This assumption clearly does not hold for all types of interventions.  As an extreme example, raising test scores
51:1916:142687:15:11:72:540:163:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:by cheating would be unlikely to yield an earnings gain of $77 per percentile improvement in test scores.  The $77
51:1917:142801:11:11:72:540:152:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:per percentile measure should be viewed as a prior estimate of the expected gain when evaluating interventions such
51:1918:142916:11:7:72:474:141:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:as class size or teacher quality for which precise estimates of earnings impacts are not yet available.
51:1919:143019:7:15:80:539:134:6:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:45This cost is obtained as follows. The annual cost of school for a child is $8,848 per year. Small classes had 15.1
51:1920:143135:15:11:72:540:119:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:students on average, while large classes had 22.56 students on average. The average small class treatment lasted 2.14
51:1921:143252:11:0:72:449:108:9:g_d0_f2:9:g_d0_f2:years. Hence, the cost per student of reducing class size is (22.56/15.1-1)*2.14*8848= $9,355.

52:1957:144833:10:10:57:540:144:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:the STAR student is claimed as a dependent.  We are unable to link 13.9% of the STAR children (and 23.9% of the U.S. cohort) to their 
52:1958:144967:10:11:57:534:134:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:parents; the summary statistics reported for parents exclude these observations.  Parent household income is average adjusted gross 
52:1959:145099:11:10:57:542:123:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:income (AGI) in years 1996-1998, when STAR participants are aged 16-18.  For years in which parents did not file, household income is 
52:1960:145233:10:10:57:544:113:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:defined as zero.  For joint-filing parents, mother's age at child's birth uses the birth date of the female parent; for single-filing parents, the 
52:1961:145380:10:10:57:549:103:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:variable uses the birth date of the single parent, who is usually female.  Other parent variables are defined in the same manner as student 
52:1962:145520:10:10:57:547:93:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:variables.  Free or reduced-price lunch eligibility is an indicator for whether the student was ever eligible during the experiment.  Student's 
52:1963:145664:10:11:57:530:83:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:age at kindergarten entry is defined as age (in days, divided by 365.25) on Sep. 1, 1985.  Teacher experience is the number of years 
52:1964:145797:11:0:57:522:72:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:taught at any school before the student's year of entry into a STAR school.  All monetary values are expressed in real 2009 dollars.
52:1965:145929:0:10:57:527:277:8:Helvetica:8:g_d0_f18:Notes: Adult outcomes, parent characteristics, and student age at KG entry are from 1996-2008 tax data; other student background 
52:1966:146058:10:10:57:541:267:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:variables and teacher characteristics are from STAR database.  Columns 1 and 2 are based on the sample of STAR students who were 
52:1967:146187:10:10:57:554:257:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:successfully linked to U.S. tax data.  Columns 3 and 4 are based on a 0.25% random sample of the U.S. population born in the same years 
52:1968:146323:10:10:57:525:247:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:as the STAR cohort (1979-80).  All available variables are defined identically in the STAR and U.S. samples.  Earnings are average 
52:1969:146454:10:11:57:529:237:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:individual earnings in years 2005-2007, measured by wage earnings on W-2 forms; those with no W-2 earnings are coded as zeros.  
52:1970:146582:11:10:57:516:226:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:College attendance is measured by receipt of a 1098-T form, issued by higher education institutions to report tuition payments or 
52:1971:146712:10:10:57:542:216:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:scholarships.    College quality is defined as the mean earnings of all former attendees of each college in the U.S. population at age 28.  
52:1972:146852:10:10:57:549:206:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:For individuals who did not attend college, college quality is defined by mean earnings at age 28 of those who did not attend college in the 
52:1973:146993:10:10:57:530:196:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:U.S. population.  Home ownership is measured as those who report mortgage interest payments on a 1040 or 1098 tax form.  401(k) 
52:1974:147121:10:11:57:551:186:8:g_d0_f18:8:g_d0_f18:contributions are reported on W-2 forms.  Marital status is measured by whether an individual files a joint tax return.  State and ZIP code of

73:2730:182904:194:13:66:545:329:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:estimates for three of the specifications.  Instrumenting with entry class dummies is ill defined in later grades 
73:2731:183018:13:12:66:543:316:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:as it would require defining class quality based purely on the new entrants, who constitute a small fraction of 
73:2732:183130:12:12:66:528:304:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:each class. Panel B replicates the specifications in A using the subsample of kindergarten entrants.  The 
73:2733:183236:12:13:66:527:292:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:leave-out mean IV estimator in Panel B Column 1 coincides with the jackknife IV of Angrist, Imbens, and 
73:2734:183340:13:12:66:540:279:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:Krueger (1995), when we use only KG entrants.  When we pool entry grades, our leave-out mean estimator 
73:2735:183443:12:12:66:516:267:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:differs from jackknife IV because we form the leave-out mean measure of class quality using all peers 
73:2736:183545:12:13:66:510:255:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:(including previous entrants), not just those who entered in the current entry grade.  Standard errors, 
73:2737:183649:13:0:66:385:242:10:g_d0_f18:10:g_d0_f18:reported in parentheses, are clustered by school in all columns except 3.

75:2825:187414:45:14:54:546:267:104:Times:104:Times:These figures plot the raw correlations between adult outcomes and kindergarten average test scores in math and reading
75:2826:187533:14:14:54:539:253:104:Times:104:Times:(measured by within-sample percentile ranks). To construct these figures, we bin test scores into twenty equal sized (5
75:2827:187652:14:13:53:534:239:104:Times:104:Times:percentile point) bins and plot the mean of the adult outcome within each bin. The solid or dashed line shows the best
75:2828:187770:13:14:53:533:226:104:Times:104:Times:linear fit estimated on the underlying student-level data using OLS. TheR2from this regression, listed in each panel,
75:2829:187887:14:14:54:549:212:104:Times:104:Times:shows how much of the variance in the outcome is explained by KG test scores. Earnings are mean annual earnings over
75:2830:188003:14:14:54:535:198:104:Times:104:Times:years 2005-2007, measured by wage earnings on W-2 forms; those with no W-2 earnings are coded as zeros. College
75:2831:188114:14:13:54:546:184:104:Times:104:Times:attendance is measured by receipt of a 1098-T form, issued by higher education institutions to report tuition payments or
75:2832:188235:13:14:54:554:171:104:Times:104:Times:scholarships, at some point between 1999 and 2007. The earnings-based index of college quality is a measure of the mean
75:2833:188354:14:14:54:547:157:104:Times:104:Times:earnings of all former attendees of each college in the U.S. population at age 28, as described in the text. For individuals
75:2834:188478:14:14:54:555:143:104:Times:104:Times:who did not attend college, college quality is defined by mean earnings at age 28 of those who did not attend college in the
75:2835:188602:14:13:54:541:129:104:Times:104:Times:U.S. population. The summary index is the standardized sum of five measures, each standardized on its own before the
75:2836:188718:13:14:54:546:116:104:Times:104:Times:sum: home ownership, 401(k) retirement savings, marital status, cross-state mobility, and percent of college graduates in
75:2837:188839:14:14:54:524:102:104:Times:104:Times:the individual’s 2007 ZIP code of residence. Thus the summary index has mean 0 and standard deviation of 1. All
75:2838:188950:14:0:54:263:88:104:Times:104:Times:monetary values are expressed in real 2009 dollars.

78:2954:193479:13:14:54:546:206:104:Times:104:Times:variable in Panel (a) is the student’s own test score at the end of the grade in which he entered STAR. The coefficient of

78:2959:194080:14:14:54:549:137:104:Times:104:Times:on class quality is $57.6 (s.e.$16.2), implying that a 1 percentile improvement in class quality leads to a $57.6 increase
